Positions on Human Action: Mu'tazila, Jabriyya, and Ahl al-Sunna
The author states three theological positions on human action and defends the Ash'ari kasb against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological positions on human action—Mu'tazila, Jabriyya, and Ahl al-Sunna—and the Ash'ari solution of kasb to reconcile divine sovereignty with human responsibility.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb resolves the tension by affirming God as Creator and the servant as acquirer in different respects.

This lesson introduces the classic debate on human agency. You will read the author's concise summary of the Mu'tazila and Jabriyya extremes, then see how Ahl al-Sunna steers a middle course using the concept of kasb. A powerful objection—how can one act be under two powers?—is answered with a nuanced distinction between creation and acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the three schools and the vivid metaphor of milk, dung, and blood.
The metaphor emphasizes the purity of the Sunni position.
2
Identify the three positions
List the Mu'tazila, Jabriyya, and Ahl al-Sunna views from the text. Note why the Mu'tazila are not declared unbelievers.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection claims one act cannot be under two powers. Find how the author responds using the two respects: creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb).
This is the core of the Ash'ari solution.
4
Reflect on the kasb doctrine
Consider how kasb preserves divine sovereignty while allowing human responsibility. Contrast with the Mu'tazila and Jabriyya positions.
Kasb is a non-causal relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب المعتزلة : وهو أن العبد خالق لأفعاله الاختيارية بقدرةٍ خَلَقَها الله فيه، ولقولهم بقدرة خلقها الله فيه لم يكفروا على الأصح، فالجبرية أفرطوا، والمعتزلة فرَّطوا ، وتوسط أهل السنة، وخيرُ الأمور أوساطها ، فخرج مذهبهم من بين فرث أعني مذهب المعتزلة، ودم أعني مذهب الجبرية لبناً خالصاً سائغاً للشاربين. فإن قيل : قد قام البرهان على وجوب استقلاله تعالى بالأفعال، والمقدور الواحد لا يدخل تحت قدرتين كما يستلزمه إثباتكم للعبد كسباً . أجيب بأنه لما ثبت بالبرهان أن الخالق هو الله سبحانه وتعالى، وبالضرورة أن لقدرة العبد مدخلاً في بعض الأفعال كحركة البطش دون البعض كحركة الارتعاش . . احتجنا في التخلص عن هذا المضيق بأنَّ الله خالق للفعل، لكن للعبد في الاختياري منه كسب، والمقدورُ الواحد يدخل تحت قدرتين بجهتين مختلفتين، فيدخل تحت قدرة الله تعالى بجهة الخلق، وتحت قدرة العبد بجهة الكسب.
And the doctrine of the Mu'tazila: that the servant is the creator of his voluntary actions by a power which God created in him, and because they say 'by a power God created in him' they are not declared unbelievers according to the most correct view. So the Jabriyya went to excess, and the Mu'tazila fell short, and the Ahl al-Sunna took the middle path, and the best of affairs are their middle courses. So their doctrine emerged from between dung—I mean the doctrine of the Mu'tazila—and blood—I mean the doctrine of the Jabriyya—as pure milk, palatable to drinkers. For if it is said: The proof has been established for the necessity of His independence, exalted is He, in actions, and a single act of that which is brought about cannot fall under two powers as required by your affirming for the servant an acquisition (kasb). The answer is: When it was established by proof that the Creator is God, exalted is He, and by necessity that the servant's power has an entry in some actions, like the movement of striking, not in others, like the movement of trembling, we needed to escape from this strait by [affirming] that God is the Creator of the act, but the servant has an acquisition (kasb) in the voluntary one, and a single act of that which is brought about falls under two powers in two different respects: it falls under the power of God, exalted is He, in the respect of creation (khalq), and under the power of the servant in the respect of acquisition (kasb).
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the three theological positions on human action: the Mu'tazila (who say the servant creates his own actions by a power created by God—note that they are not declared unbelievers due to their affirmation of a created power), the Jabriyya (who deny all human agency), and the Ahl al-Sunna (the middle path). The commentator then cites a vivid metaphor: the Sunna's doctrine emerges pure from between the 'dung' of Mu'tazilism and the 'blood' of Jabrism. A hypothetical objection is raised: how can you affirm acquisition for the servant when the proof shows God alone is independent in actions, and one event cannot be produced by two agents? The answer follows: experience shows that some actions (like voluntary striking) involve the servant's power, while others (like trembling) do not. The solution is to say God creates the act, but the servant has 'kasb' (acquisition) in it—a relationship that allows a single act to be under two powers in different respects: creation (khalq) from God, acquisition (kasb) from the servant. This preserves divine sovereignty while accounting for human responsibility.

Key Terms
كسب
قدرة
خلق
جبرية
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans create their own actions.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, humans do not create; they acquire (kasb), which is a non-causal relation that does not involve creating.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how does the Ash'ari doctrine resolve the problem of a single act being under two powers?
A) By denying the servant has any role at all.
B) By asserting the act falls under God's power in respect of creation and under the servant's power in respect of acquisition.
C) By claiming the act is partly created by God and partly by the servant.
D) By saying the act is created by the servant and only sustained by God.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari solution to the problem of human action is to distinguish between creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb), allowing a single act to be attributed to both God and the servant in different respects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, when you sin, do you truly feel that God created that action, yet you are fully accountable? How does that knowledge change how you approach your own faults—do you blame yourself or God? Are you willing to own your sin completely while still acknowledging God's sovereignty?
?
This lesson distinguishes between voluntary actions (like striking) and involuntary ones (like trembling). Look at your own choices today: which of your actions are truly voluntary and thus ‘acquired’ by you? Are you using your God-given power to obey, or are you passively excusing your disobedience as ‘God’s will’? Commit to one specific area where you will act consciously as an acquirer.
?
The metaphor of pure milk coming from dung and blood reveals the subtlety of the middle path. Are you tempted to simplify the tension—either by denying your responsibility (like the Jabriyya) or by claiming too much independence (like the Mu'tazila)? Where in your heart do you lean to one side, and what step will you take today to stand in the truth of both God’s creation and your acquisition?

Diverse Ash'ari Views on Kasb
The commentator surveys five Ash'ari scholars on kasb and clarifies the scope of disagreement with Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of Ash'ari positions on how acquired actions relate to divine power and created capacity.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari school is not monolithic on kasb—positions range from non-influence to conditional effect.

This passage surveys several key Ash'ari scholars and their specific formulations of kasb (acquisition). Each scholar attempted to explain how a human action can be both freely chosen and entirely dependent on God. The diversity of their views shows that the issue was not settled by a single formula but remained a subject of nuanced theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five scholars
Read the passage and list the scholars mentioned: al-Maturidi, al-Sha'rani, al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), and al-Juwayni. Note the distinct view attributed to each.
Al-Maturidi is actually a founder of the Maturidi school, but he is included here as part of the broader discussion.
2
Understand the spectrum of positions
Observe how views progress from al-Maturidi's rejection of Jabriyya (no actual act for servant) through al-Ash'ari's non‑influence (power attached as knowledge) to al-Juwayni's conditional effect (power influences its locus on condition of connection).
Al-Qadi's view of kasb as a 'state' (hal) is intermediate, avoiding both causality and independence.
3
Connect to the broader disagreement
The passage ends by clarifying that the only area of debate with Mu'tazila is voluntary human actions under manifest power. All other events are uncontroversially from God.
This shows that the discussion is limited in scope, not about all events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر الماتريدي في التوحيد» (ص) (۳۱۹) أن الجبرية أرجعوا جميع الأفعال إلى الله، ولم يُثبتوا للعباد في التحقيق فعلاً . وقال العلامة الشعراني في اليواقيت : العبد بين طرفي الاضطرار، مضطر على الاختيار)، وقال أيضاً : قال الشيخ الأشعري : تعلق القدرة الحادثة كتعلق العلم بالمعلوم من عدم التأثير). وقد جعل القاضي الكسب حالاً؛ لأنه لا فرق بين الحركتين إلا بحال هي تفرقة موجودة في النفس . والذي قال بتأثير القدرة الحادثة كالجويني أسند أسبابها إلى قدرة الله، وإن لم يثبت للعبد استقلالاً، فقد صرح الجويني بتأثير القدرة الحادثة من مدارك العقول، وأنها تؤثر في محلها على شرط الاتصال، ومثل بالعبد الذي ماله بإذن سيده في البيع . ثم موضوع الخلاف بين أهل السنة والمعتزلة هو أفعال العباد التي تدخل تحت قدرتهم الظاهرة، وما سوى ذلك فلا خلاف عند المعتزلة يكونه من عند الله استقلالاً. انظر «شرح المقاصد». وهذا يقتضي أن مثل ذلك من محل الخلاف فلينظر .
And al-Maturidi mentioned in al-Tawhid (p. 319) that the Jabriyya referred all actions to God and did not affirm for the servants any actual act. And the scholar al-Sha'rani said in al-Yawaqit: 'The servant is between two extremes of compulsion, compelled by choice.' And he also said: 'Al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari said: The attachment of the originated power is like the attachment of knowledge to the known in terms of non-influence.' And al-Qadi made kasb a state (hal) because there is no difference between the two movements except a state which is a distinction existing in the soul. And whoever said about the influence of the originated power like al-Juwayni attributed its causes to the power of God, even if he did not affirm independence for the servant. Verily al-Juwayni explicitly affirmed the influence of the originated power from the perceptions of intellects, and that it influences its locus on condition of connection, and he gave the example of the servant whose property is with the permission of his master in selling. Then the subject of disagreement between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila is the actions of servants that fall under their apparent power; apart from that there is no disagreement with the Mu'tazila that they are from God independently. See Sharh al-Maqasid. And this requires that such belongs to the locus of disagreement, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage surveys prominent Ash'ari and related scholars and their specific views on kasb and created power. First, al-Maturidi (founder of the Maturidi school) is cited stating that the Jabriyya (extreme predestinarians) deny any actual act to servants, a position he rejects. Then al-Sha'rani (a later Ash'ari scholar) is quoted with a paradoxical statement: 'The servant is between two extremes of compulsion, compelled by choice'—an attempt to capture the mystery of predestination and free will. Al-Sha'rani also attributes to al-Ash'ari the comparison of originated power's relationship to its object (the act) to the relationship of knowledge to its known object: neither has any causal effect; they simply accompany. Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) is reported as making kasb a 'state' (hal), an ontological category that is neither existent nor non-existent, to explain the lack of difference between two identical movements (one voluntary, one involuntary) except for a mental distinction. Al-Juwayni takes a slightly stronger position, asserting that the originated power does have an effect (ta'thir) on its locus, but only on condition of connection (i.e., with God's permission), and he analogizes it to a servant whose property is sold with the master's permission—the servant acts, but the permission is essential. Finally, the commentator clarifies the subject of disagreement between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila: it concerns only the voluntary actions of servants that fall under their manifest power. All other events are uncontroversially from God alone. The note ends with a reference to Sharh al-Maqasid (a commentary by al-Taftazani) and a remark that this issue is indeed a point of difference.

Correction: This passage shows a spectrum: from al-Ash'ari's non-influence to al-Juwayni's conditional effect, with al-Qadi's 'state' in between.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school contains a range of explanations for kasb, from non‑influence to conditional effect, but all affirm that God is the sole creator of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Sha'rani says you are 'compelled by choice.' Think of a recent action you deeply regretted. In that moment, did you feel you could have done otherwise? What does this paradox reveal about your accountability before Allah? Today, when you pray or sin, will you own your choice while knowing God is its ultimate creator?
?
Al-Juwayni's example of a servant selling with the master's permission shows your deed is real but entirely contingent. When you think of your greatest achievement or worst failure, do you truly feel that every detail depended on God's permission? Or do you slip into thinking you are independent? What would change in your prayer and gratitude if you lived this moment by moment?
?
The passage limits the debate with Mu'tazila to voluntary acts. That means your everyday choices—the words you speak, the food you eat—are the frontier of this doctrine. Does this raise the stakes for small decisions? How can you use this awareness to turn every action into an act of reliance on God and reflection on His sovereignty?

Objection on Inward Compulsion and Its Answer
Objection that inward compulsion negates outward choice and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that inward compulsion negates outward choice is answered through the distinction between reality (haqiqa) and Sharia.

Core Idea
The servant is compelled inwardly but appears free outwardly — two perspectives, one truth.

This lesson presents a classic objection about divine determinism and human responsibility. The objector argues that if all actions are compelled by God, then external choice is meaningless. The answer draws on a profound Sufi insight: reality sees the divine hand, Sharia sees human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objection is framed: if inward compulsion is true, then outward choice becomes meaningless. Note the three premises: divine foreknowledge, creation of power, inevitability of act.
2
Examine the answer
The answer begins with a strong theological principle: God is not questioned. Then it introduces the Sufi distinction between the eye of reality and the eye of Sharia.
3
Understand the dual perspective
The quote from Ibrahim al-Dasuqi shows that from haqiqa we excuse people, from Sharia we blame. This resolves the tension.
4
Reflect on Sufi inward compulsion
The passage clarifies that Sufis do not teach outward compulsion (which would negate moral responsibility) but inward compulsion — the secret reality behind apparent choice.
5
Connect to refutation of Mu'tazila
By denying the servant's real effect (ta'thir), the passage rejects the Mu'tazila who affirm human independent creation of acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا كان مجبوراً باطناً فلا معنى للاختيار الظاهري؛ لأنَّ الله قد علم وقوع الفعل ولا بدَّ، وخلق في العبد القدرة عليه. أجيب بأنه تعالى لا يُسأل عما يفعل ; ولذلك قال سيدي إبراهيم الدسوقي: مَنْ نظر للخلق بعين الحقيقة عَذَرَهم ، ومَنْ نظر لهم بعين الشريعة مقتهم، فالعبد مجبور في صورة مختار. والصوفية يشيرون للجبر كثيراً، وحاشاهم من الجبر الظاهري، وإنَّما مرادهم الجبر الباطنيُّ ; ويُفهم من نفي التأثير ردُّ مذهب المعتزلة .
If it is said: If he is inwardly compelled, then there is no meaning for outward choice; because Allah has already known the occurrence of the action inevitably, and He created in the servant the power over it. The answer is that He (Most High) is not questioned about what He does. Therefore, my master Ibrahim al-Dasuqi said: 'Whoever looks at creation with the eye of reality (al-ḥaqīqa) excuses them, and whoever looks at them with the eye of the Sharia condemns them.' So the servant is compelled in the form of one who chooses. And the Sufis often refer to compulsion, but they are far from outward compulsion; rather, they mean inward compulsion. And from the negation of effectiveness is understood the refutation of the Muʿtazila.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if the servant is inwardly compelled (i.e., God creates all acts, including the power and the choice), then outward choice is meaningless because God's knowledge and creation determine the act. The answer is that God is not questioned about His actions — His decree is beyond human questioning. The author then quotes Ibrahim al-Dasuqi to illustrate that from the perspective of spiritual truth (ḥaqīqa), one sees the divine determinism and excuses people, but from the legal perspective (sharīʿa), one holds them accountable. Thus the servant is compelled in reality but appears as a chooser before the law. The Sufis often speak of compulsion inwardly, not outwardly. Finally, denying that the servant has any real effect (taʾthīr) refutes the Mu'tazila who affirm that humans independently create their own acts.

Correction: The answer is based on a deep theological principle and a distinction between two valid perspectives. Sufis explicitly reject outward compulsion; they affirm inward compulsion as the hidden reality behind apparent choice.

The Servant's Freedom: Neither Compelled Nor Without Choice
Explanation of verse 49 affirming the middle path on human choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the servant has a form of choice (ikhtiyār) through kasb, refuting both Jabriyya (compulsion) and Qadariyya (absolute free will).

Core Idea
The servant's choice is real but not creative; it is a capacity to will and act within God's creation.

This lesson examines verse 49 of the poem, which succinctly states the Ash'ari middle path on human action. The verse asserts that the servant is neither compelled like a stone nor endowed with absolute free will. The commentary clarifies the meaning of 'nor choice' as a double negative affirming a real but derived choice. Understanding this verse is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari theory of kasb and its refutation of extremist views.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its literal translation
Start by reading the Arabic verse (fa-laysa majbūran ...) and its English translation. Get a feel for its rhythm and structure before diving into analysis.
The verse is a single line with two negations.
2
Understand 'wa-lā ikhtiyārā' as explanatory apposition
The commentary explains that 'wa-lā ikhtiyārā' is not an independent negation but an explanatory apposition of 'majbūran'. This means it rephrases the first negation: 'not compelled' is equivalent to 'not without choice'. So the verse affirms that the servant has choice.
This grammatical point is key to unlocking the meaning.
3
See how this refutes Jabriyya and Qadariyya
The author explicitly states his purpose is to refute the Jabriyya, who deny any human choice. At the same time, the verse implicitly refutes the Qadariyya (and Mu'tazila) who affirm absolute free will, because the choice affirmed is not independent of God's creation.
The Ash'ari middle path: the servant has kasb, not creation.
4
Connect to the broader concept of kasb
Recall that the preceding verses established that the servant has kasb in voluntary acts. This verse builds on that: because of kasb, the servant is not compelled, and he has a real (though non-creative) choice.
Kasb is the acquisition of the act; God creates the act, the servant 'acquires' it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٩ - فَلَيْسَ مَجْبُوراً وَلَا اخْتِيَارَا وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اخْتِبَارَا قوله: (فَلَيْسَ مَجْبُوراً ... إلخ) أي: إذا علمت أن للعبد كسباً في أفعاله الاختيارية .. فاعتقد أنَّ العبد ليس مجبوراً. وقوله : (وَلَا اخْتِيَارَا) عطف تفسير لمعنى مجبوراً ، فكأنَّه قال: (أي: لا اختيار له في صدور أفعاله عنه، وهو مُسَلَّط عليه النفي السابق ; فالمراد أنه ليس (لا اختيار له)، بل له اختيار (۲) ، وغرض المصنفِ بذلك التصريح بالرد على الجبرية في قولهم :
49 - So he is not compelled, nor choice, and not everyone acts by choice? (verse). His statement: (fa-laysa majbūran ... etc.) i.e., when you know that the servant has kash in his voluntary actions... then believe that the servant is not compelled. And His statement: (wa-lā ikhtiyārā) is an explanatory apposition to the meaning of 'majbūran', so it is as if he said: (i.e., no choice for him in the issuance of his actions from him, and he is subjected to the previous negation; so the intended meaning is that he is not 'no choice for him', rather he has choice (2), and the author's purpose by that is to explicitly refute the Jabriyya in their saying:
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage explains verse 49 of the poem. The verse states: 'So he is not compelled, nor choice, and not everyone acts by choice.' The commentary clarifies that the negation of compulsion does not mean affirming absolute freedom; rather, the phrase 'wa-lā ikhtiyārā' is an explanatory apposition to 'majbūran', meaning that the servant is not without choice. In other words, he is neither compelled (like a stone) nor does he have absolute free will (as Mu'tazila claim). Instead, he has kash and an outward choice (ikhtiyār ẓāhirī), which is a capacity to will and act, though God creates the act. The author's aim is to refute the Jabriyya who say the servant has no choice at all. The passage is cut off but the basic Ash'ari position is clear: the servant has a form of choice (though not creative) that grounds moral responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse says the servant has no choice because it negates both compulsion and choice.
Correction: The phrase 'wa-lā ikhtiyārā' is not an independent negation; it is an explanatory apposition to 'majbūran', meaning 'not without choice', so it actually affirms a type of choice.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'fa-laysa majbūran wa-lā ikhtiyārā' affirms that the servant has a real choice (ikhtiyār) through kasb, neither compelled nor absolutely free.

The Servant's Lack of Influence and Inward Compulsion
The commentator states the servant's lack of influence, then answers an objection about compulsion and choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that the servant has no real influence but possesses outward choice, and how this reconciles divine omnipotence with human accountability.

Core Idea
The servant is compelled inwardly but free outwardly — a mystery of divine decree and human responsibility.

This passage tackles one of the most delicate topics in Islamic theology: the relationship between God's omnipotence and human action. The commentator states the Ash'ari position directly, then addresses a common objection. As you read, pay attention to how the author distinguishes between inward reality and outward Sharia perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words sink in before interpreting.
Notice the concise style — every phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the three key claims
The passage makes three foundational claims: (1) the servant has no influence, (2) he is inwardly compelled, (3) he is outwardly free. Find these in the text.
These three form the core of the Ash'ari position on human action.
3
Understand the objection and answer
An imaginary objector argues that inward compulsion makes outward choice meaningless. The answer references God's sovereignty. Work through the logic.
The answer 'He is not questioned' is a key Ash'ari principle.
4
Reflect on the Sufi perspective
The quote from al-Disuqi shows how the same truth looks from two angles: reality (haqiqa) and Sharia. This illustrates the compatibility of inward compulsion with outward accountability.
The Sufis often speak of compulsion, but they mean inward, not outward.
5
Note the refutation of Mu'tazilism
The passage ends by explicitly refuting the Mu'tazilite view that humans create their own actions. This clarifies the doctrinal stakes.
This shows how the passage fits into broader kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فليس للعبد تأثير ما ، فهو مجبور باطناً، مختار ظاهراً . فإن قيل : إذا كان مجبوراً باطناً فلا معنى للاختيار الظاهري؛ لأنَّ الله قد علم وقوع الفعل ولا بدَّ، وخلق في العبد القدرة عليه. أجيب بأنه تعالى لا يُسأل عما يفعل ؛ ولذلك قال سيدي إبراهيم الدسوقي: مَنْ نظر للخلق بعين الحقيقة عَذَرَهم ، ومَنْ نظر لهم بعين الشريعة مقتهم، فالعبد مجبور في صورة مختار. والصوفية يشيرون للجبر كثيراً، وحاشاهم من الجبر الظاهري، وإنَّما مرادهم الجبر الباطنيُّ ؛ ويُفهم من نفي التأثير ردُّ مذهب المعتزلة .
In summary, the servant has no influence at all. So he is compelled inwardly, free outwardly. For if it is said: If he is compelled inwardly, then outward choice has no meaning, because God has known the occurrence of the action necessarily, and has created in the servant the ability to do it. The answer is that He (exalted) is not questioned about what He does. And for this reason Sayyid Ibrahim al-Disuqi said: Whoever looks at creation with the eye of reality excuses them, and whoever looks at them with the eye of the Sharia hates them. So the servant is compelled in the form of one who chooses. And the Sufis allude to compulsion often, but far be it from them to intend outward compulsion; rather they mean inward compulsion. And from the negation of influence, one understands a refutation of the Mu'tazilite school.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument and objection-and-answer. It first states the Ash'ari position that the servant has no real influence (tathir) in his actions; he is inwardly compelled (majbur batinan) but outwardly free (mukhtar zahiran). Then an objection is raised: if he is compelled inwardly, outward choice is meaningless, since God's knowledge necessitates the act and He creates the ability. The answer is that God is not questioned about what He does—a reference to absolute divine sovereignty. The commentator then cites Ibrahim al-Disuqi to show that from the perspective of ultimate reality (haqiqa), all acts are from God, so excuses are granted; from the perspective of Sharia, blame is placed. This leads to the famous Sufi saying: the servant is compelled in the form of one who chooses. The passage ends by noting that negating influence refutes the Mu'tazilites, who hold that humans create their own actions.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the servant is outwardly free and held accountable; inward compulsion does not negate Sharia responsibility.

The Ash'ari middle path is that the servant has no real influence but exercises outward choice, being compelled in the form of one who chooses, thereby upholding both divine sovereignty and Sharia responsibility.

?

If not, what is holding you back from surrendering that area of your heart? Ask Allah to grant you the unveiling of His sole agency while keeping you firm in your duties.

The Servant Is Not Compelled nor Autonomous
Commentary on first line refuting Jabriyya with poetic exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on human action, affirming kasb (acquisition) and rejecting both pure compulsion (Jabr) and pure independence (Tafwid).

Core Idea
The servant is neither a feather in the wind nor a creator of his actions.

The commentator explains the first line of the poem, which denies both extreme views. He clarifies that the servant has kasb (acquisition) and apparent choice, and he refutes the Jabriyya who claim total compulsion. A poetic exchange illustrates the folly of the Jabri argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first line and its explanation
Start by reading the Arabic line 'falis majburan wa la ikhtiyara' and its commentary. Notice how the commentator immediately connects kasb to the denial of compulsion.
The poet is not teaching fatalism; he is setting a middle path.
2
Identify the two extreme views
The poet denies two things: being compelled (majbur) and having pure choice (ikhtiyar). Understand that 'compelled' means no choice, and 'pure choice' means absolute independence. The commentator explains that the intended meaning is that the servant is not without choice.
3
Understand the refutation of the Jabriyya
The Jabriyya claim the servant has no choice at all, like a feather in the wind. The commentator explicitly states that the author's purpose is to refute them by affirming kasb.
Kasb is the key concept that preserves both divine power and human responsibility.
4
Analyze the poetic exchange
Read the Jabri poet's couplet and the Sunni response. The Jabri analogy is absurd: throwing someone bound into the sea and telling them not to get wet. The Sunni response shows that divine decree is consistent with human action; safety or drowning depends on God's will, not location.
This exchange vividly illustrates the logical consequence of pure Jabr: it makes God seem unjust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٩ - فَلَيْسَ مَجْبُوراً وَلَا اخْتِيَارَا وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اخْتِبَارَا قوله: (فَلَيْسَ مَجْبُوراً ... إلخ) أي: إذا علمت أن للعبد كسباً في أفعاله الاختيارية .. فاعتقد أنَّ العبد ليس مجبوراً. وقوله : (وَلَا اخْتِيَارَا) عطف تفسير لمعنى مجبوراً ، فكأنَّه قال: (أي: لا اختيار له في صدور أفعاله عنه، وهو مُسَلَّط عليه النفي السابق ؛ فالمراد أنه ليس (لا اختيار له)، بل له اختيار ، وغرض المصنفِ بذلك التصريح بالرد على الجبرية في قولهم : إنَّ العبد مجبورٌ لا اختيار له في صدور جميع أفعاله عنه، فهو كريشة معلقة في الهواء تميلها الرياح يميناً وشمالاً ، قال شاعرهم مورداً على أهل السنة : [من البسيط] مَا حِيْلَةُ العَبْدِ وَالأَقْدَارُ جَارِيَةٌ عَلَيْهِ فِي كُلِّ حَالٍ أَيُّهَا الرَّائِي؟ أَلْقَاهُ فِي اليَمِّ مَكْتُوفاً وَقَالَ لَهُ: إِيَّاكَ إِيَّاكَ أَنْ تَبْتَلَّ بِالمَاءِ وأجابه بعض أهل السنة بقوله : [من البسيط] إنْ حَقَّهُ اللُّطْفُ لَمْ يَمْسَسْهُ مِنْ بَلَلٍ وَلَمْ يُبَالِ بِتَكْتِيفِ وَالْقَاءِ وَإِنْ يَكُنْ قَدَرُ المَوْلَى بِغَرْقَتِهِ فَهْوَ الفَرِيقُ وَلَوْ أُلْقِي بِصَحْرَاءِ قوله : (وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اخْتِيَارَا) أي : وليس العبد يفعل كل فعل حال كون ذلك الكسب أثبت الاختيار الظاهري، ومن نفاه - وهم الجبرية - نفى الاختيار الظاهري.
49. So he is neither compelled nor possessing pure choice, nor does he do everything by choice. His saying: 'so he is not compelled... etc.' means: when you know that the servant has acquisition (kasb) in his voluntary acts, then believe that the servant is not compelled. And his saying 'nor possessing choice' is an explanatory conjunction to the meaning of 'compelled', so it is as if he said: 'i.e., he has no choice in the occurrence of his actions from him', and the previous negation is reinforced upon it; but the intended meaning is that he is not 'having no choice', rather he has choice. And the author's purpose in that is to explicitly refute the Jabriyya in their saying: 'The servant is compelled, having no choice in the occurrence of all his actions from him; he is like a feather suspended in the air, moved by winds right and left.' Their poet said, addressing the Ahl al-Sunnah: 'What stratagem has the servant when the decrees flow over him in every state, O beholder? He threw him into the sea bound and said to him: Beware, beware of getting wet with water.' And a certain Ahl al-Sunnah answered him: 'If divine gentleness is his due, no wetness touches him, and he cares not for being bound and thrown; and if the Master's decree is to drown him, then that is the outcome even if he is thrown into a desert.' His saying: 'Nor does he do everything by choice' means: and the servant does not do every act while that kasb establishes apparent choice, and whoever negates it – and they are the Jabriyya – has negated apparent choice.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of the poem (al-Rajaz). It begins by explaining the first line: the servant is neither compelled (majbur) nor possessing independent choice (la ikhtiyara) in the sense of having no choice at all; rather he has kasb and apparent choice. The author's aim is to refute the Jabriyya, who claim the servant has absolutely no choice, like a feather in the wind. The commentator then quotes a Jabri poet's couplet mocking the Sunni position: the servant is bound and thrown into the sea yet told not to get wet. The Sunni answer is that if divine grace (al-lutf) decrees safety, no harm comes; if drowning is decreed, it will happen regardless of location. This preserves both divine decree and human accountability. Finally, the commentator explains the second line: the servant does not do everything by choice; kasb establishes an apparent choice, which the Jabriyya deny. This lesson emphasizes the middle path between Jabr and Qadr.

Key Terms
جبرية
اختيار
كسب
مجبور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the poet is teaching that humans have no choice at all, similar to the Jabriyya.
Correction: The poet denies both pure compulsion and pure independence; the commentator affirms that the servant has choice through kasb.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main purpose of the author in writing this line?
A) To affirm that the servant has no choice.
B) To refute the Jabriyya by affirming kasb and apparent choice.
C) To support the Qadariyya.
D) To deny divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The first line denies both pure compulsion and pure autonomy, establishing the middle path of kasb where the servant has a real, but not independent, choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels utterly beyond your control, do you sometimes fall into the belief that you are like a feather in the wind—completely helpless? How does this lesson's affirmation of kasb confront you to take action while trusting Allah's decree?
?
In what area of your life do you subtly act as if you have absolute control, forgetting that the ultimate outcome is from Allah alone? How would your daily decisions change if you truly internalized that you have choice but not autonomy?
?
The poet's image of being bound and thrown into the sea with the command 'do not get wet' is absurd. Where in your own thinking do you make similar excuses, blaming predestination for your failures while ignoring the real choices you made? What would it mean for you to repent and embrace the responsibility of kasb?

Apparent Choice: The Servant Does Not Do Everything by Choice
Explanation of the second line on apparent choice and refutation of Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the servant's kasb establishes an apparent choice (al-ikhtiyar al-zahiri), but he does not have complete independence in all his actions.

Core Idea
Kasb gives the servant apparent choice, not full autonomy.

The commentator explains the second line of the poem, which qualifies the first. While the servant has choice through kasb, he does not do every act by his own independent choice. This further refutes the Jabriyya who deny any human choice whatsoever.

Guided Path
1
Read the second line and its explanation
Read the Arabic 'wa laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyara' and the short commentary that follows. Notice that the servant is said to not do everything by choice.
This line balances the first: the servant has choice, but it is limited.
2
Understand 'apparent choice' (al-ikhtiyar al-zahiri)
The commentary states that kasb establishes an apparent choice. This means the servant's action is attributed to him in a real sense, but it is not independent of Allah's power. The choice is 'apparent' because it is not absolute.
3
See the contrast with the Jabriyya
The commentator explicitly says that the Jabriyya deny even this apparent choice. They negate any role for the servant, which the poet rejects.
This completes the refutation: the servant has a genuine but derived choice.

?

?

Divine Decree
intermediate

The Middle Station: Human Action Between Compulsion and Choice
Sanusi states the obligatory belief about human action, provides rational evidence, and quotes al-Ghazali to explain the middle position (kasb).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari concept of kasb as a middle position between pure compulsion and pure free will, and why it is the obligatory belief regarding human actions.

Core Idea
Human action is neither pure compulsion nor pure choice—it is compelled to choose.

This lesson addresses a central question in Islamic theology: Are human actions forced by God, or do we have free will? The Ash'ari answer, as presented by Sanusi and elaborated by al-Ghazali, is that human actions occupy a middle station (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). The concept of kasb (acquisition) captures this nuanced position. By reading the passage, you will grasp the obligatory belief and the rational evidence for it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text and the English translation side by side. Notice the key terms: اضطرار, اختيار, جبر, كسب. The passage begins with a clear statement of what must be believed.
2
Identify the rational evidence
The passage provides a self-evident distinction: the difference between a deliberate movement (like striking) and an involuntary tremor. This is the rational proof that some actions are voluntary and some are compelled.
Keep in mind that this distinction is known by every rational person, not just by scholars.
3
Understand al-Ghazali's explanation
Read the quote from al-Ghazali's Ihya carefully. He defines 'compelled' and 'free' in a precise way: compulsion means the action originates from another, freedom means one is the locus of a will that arises by compulsion after reason judges the action good. This leads to the idea that humans are 'compelled to choose'.
Al-Ghazali's language is technical; take time to see how he reconciles divine control with human responsibility.
4
Learn the term 'kasb'
The passage explains that the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) coined a third term, 'kasb,' to describe this intermediate state. Kasb is not pure compulsion nor pure free will but something that combines them. It is grounded in the Qur'an.
The word kasb literally means 'acquisition.' It signifies that humans acquire actions God creates.
5
Reflect on language about God
The passage concludes with an important caution: all language used about God, including 'choice,' is metaphorical. We must avoid attributing hesitation or uncertainty to Allah. This is a key principle in kalam.
Why is this important? Because misunderstanding theological language can lead to anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والواجب اعتقاده أنَّ بعض أفعاله صادر باختياره والبعض الآخر باضطراره، لما يجده كلُّ عاقل من الفرق الضروري بين حركة البطش وحركة المرتعش. وقد قال الحجة الغزالي في إحيائه: (معنى كونه مجبوراً أن جميع ذلك حاصل فيه من غيره لا منه، ومعنى كونه مختاراً أنه محل لإرادة حدثت فيه جبراً بعد حكم العقل بكون الفعل خيراً محضاً موافقاً، وحدث الحكم أيضاً جبراً، فإذا هو مجبور على الاختيار، ففعل النار في الإحراق مثلاً جبر محض وفعل الله تعالى اختيار محض وفعل الإنسان على منزلة بين المنزلتين ؛ فإنه جبر على الاختيار، فطلب أهل الحق لهذا عبارة ثالثة ؛ لأنه لما كان فناً ثالثاً واتموا فيه بكتاب الله تعالى، فسموه كسباً، وليس مناقضاً للجبر ولا للاختيار، بل هو جامع بينهما عند من فهمه، وفعل الله تعالى يسمى اختياراً بشرط ألا يُفهم من الاختيار إرادة بعد تحير وتردد، فإن ذلك في حقه محال، وجميع الألفاظ المذكورة في اللغات لا يمكن أن تُستعمل في حق الله تعالى إلا على نوع من الاستعارة والتجوز، وذكر ذلك لا يليق بهذا العلم ويطول القول فيه).
And what must be believed is that some of his actions occur by his choice, and others by necessity. Due to what every rational person finds of the necessary distinction between the movement of deliberate striking and the movement of the one trembling. And al-Ghazali said in his Ihya: 'The meaning of his being compelled is that all of that occurs in him from other than him, not from him; and the meaning of his being free is that he is a locus for an will that occurs in him by compulsion after the judgment of reason that the action is purely good and suitable, and the judgment also occurs by compulsion, so he is compelled to choose. So the action of fire in burning, for example, is pure compulsion, and the action of God is pure choice, and the human action is a station between the two stations; for he is compelled to choose. So the people of truth sought a third expression for this; because it was a third kind, and they followed in it the Book of God, so they called it kasb, and it is not contrary to compulsion nor to choice, rather it combines them in the understanding of whoever understands it. And the action of God is called choice on condition that choice is not understood as will after bewilderment and hesitation, for that is impossible with respect to Him; and all the mentioned words in languages cannot be used with respect to God except in a metaphorical and figurative sense, and discussing that is not suitable for this science and would be lengthy.'
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage states the obligatory belief (al-wajib i'tiqaduhu) that some human actions are voluntary and some involuntary, as evidenced by the self-evident difference between a deliberate action (like striking) and an involuntary tremor. This is the basis for the Ash'ari concept of kasb. The commentator then quotes al-Ghazali's Ihya to elaborate: the human being is compelled to choose; his will occurs in him by compulsion from God, but he is still the locus of that will. Thus human action is neither pure compulsion (like fire) nor pure choice (like God's), but a middle station (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). The term kasb captures this. The passage notes that all language about God is metaphorical and that applying human concepts of choice to God requires negation of imperfection (such as hesitation).

Key Terms
اضطراري
اختياري
جبر
اختيار
كسب
منزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'compelled to choose' is a contradiction or that it means humans have no real responsibility.
Correction: The passage explains that the human being is the locus of a will that is created by God, but the action is still attributed to the human as 'acquisition' (kasb). It is not pure compulsion like fire, nor pure free will like God's, but a middle position that preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what is the nature of human action?
A) Pure compulsion like the action of fire.
B) Pure choice like the action of God.
C) A station between compulsion and choice.
D) Neither compulsion nor choice.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are neither purely compelled nor purely free; they occupy a middle station (kasb) where the action is created by God but acquired by the human.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision you made that you felt was entirely your own. Can you now trace it back to a judgment of reason that came to you without your control? How does recognizing that your reasoning itself is compelled by God change how you view your responsibility for that choice? Before you sleep tonight, pause over one such decision and ask God to forgive you for the pride you may have felt in 'your' reasoning.
?
Al-Ghazali warns against imagining God's choice as involving deliberation or hesitation. When you think about God's will, do you ever privately imagine it as similar to your own decision-making process? That thought is a subtle form of tashbih. What one concrete practice can you adopt today to purify your understanding of God's attributes from human-like qualities?
?
The term 'compelled to choose' can feel uncomfortable because it challenges our sense of control. In which area of your life do you most resist surrendering the illusion of absolute free will? Name it now in your heart. Write it down and then read the Arabic phrase 'مجبور على الاختيار' aloud. Let it sink in that your very will to choose is a gift from God. Will you commit to thanking Him for each decision you make tomorrow, even small ones, as a sign of trust in His creation of your choices?

The Jabriyya Objection and the Sunni Response
Poetic refutation of Jabriyya determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme Jabriyya position as illustrated by a poem and the Sunni response affirming divine wisdom and human responsibility.

Core Idea
God's decree does not negate human responsibility; the Jabriyya comparison to a bird is flawed.

This lesson presents a poetic exchange between the Jabriyya and the People of the Sunnah. The Jabriyya poet argues that humans have no choice, like a feather in the wind. The Sunni poet responds by emphasizing that God's decree is just and that human actions remain meaningful within it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic poem
Read the Arabic passage once without translating. Notice the imagery of the feather and the bound man thrown into the sea.
2
Identify the Jabriyya objection
The Jabriyya claim that the servant has no device against the decrees. Understand their deterministic view.
3
Analyze the Sunni response
The Sunni poet does not deny the decree but argues that if God wants mercy, no harm will come; if He wants drowning, it happens regardless. This affirms God's absolute will and the servant's dependency.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how this response preserves both divine omnipotence and human responsibility without falling into pure compulsion.
This sets the stage for the rational proof in the next lesson.

إنَّ العبد مجبورٌ لا اختيار له في صدور جميع أفعاله عنه، فهو كريشة معلقة في الهواء تميلها الرياح يميناً وشمالاً ، قال شاعرهم مورداً على أهل السنة : [من البسيط] مَا حِيْلَةُ العَبْدِ وَالأَقْدَارُ جَارِيَةٌ عَلَيْهِ فِي كُلِّ حَالٍ أَيُّهَا الرَّائِي؟ أَلْقَاهُ فِي اليَمِّ مَكْتُوفاً وَقَالَ لَهُ: إِيَّاكَ إِيَّاكَ أَنْ تَبْتَلَّ بِالمَاءِ وأجابه بعض أهل السنة (١) بقوله : [من البسيط] إنْ حَقَّهُ اللُّطْفُ لَمْ يَمْسَسْهُ مِنْ بَلَلٍ وَلَمْ يُبَالِ بِتَكْتِيفِ وَالْقَاءِ وَإِنْ يَكُنْ قَدَرُ المَوْلَى بِغَرْقَتِهِ فَهْوَ الفَرِيقُ وَلَوْ أُلْقِي بِصَحْرَاءِ والواجب اعتقاده أنَّ بعض أفعاله صادر باختياره والبعض الآخر باضطراره، لما يجده كلُّ عاقل من الفرق الضروري بين حركة البطش وحركة المرتعش (٢). الكسب أثبت الاختيار الظاهري، ومن نفاه - وهم الجبرية - نفى الاختيار الظاهري. أجهوري.
(۱) قوله : (أجابه بعض أهل السنة إلخ) وأجاب بعضهم أيضاً بقوله :
لا يُسأل الله عن عن أفعاله أبداً فهو الحكيم بـحـرمـان وإعطاء
yخص بالفضل أقواماً فيرحمهم وضدُّ ذلك لا يخفى على الرائي
انتهى
قوله : (أيضاً وأجابه بعض أهل السنة إلخ) هذا الجواب لا يظهر بل كان الظاهر الجواب بالتفرقة بين المكلف وبين من ألقي في اليم مكتوفاً، ونُهي عن أن يبتل بأن للمكلف اختياراً ظاهراً وكسباً، ولا كذلك المكتوف. انتهى أجهوري .
-
(۲) فقد قال الحجة الغزالي في إحيائه» (٢٥٥٤): (معنى كونه مجبوراً أن جميع ذلك حاصل فيه من غيره لا منه، ومعنى كونه مختاراً أنه محل لإرادة حدثت فيه جبراً بعد حكم العقل بكون الفعل خيراً محضاً موافقاً، وحدث الحكم أيضاً جبراً، فإذا هو مجبور على الاختيار، ففعل النار في الإحراق مثلاً جبر ،محض وفعل الله تعالى اختيار ،محض وفعل الإنسان على منزلة بين المنزلتين ؛ فإنه جبر على الاختيار، فطلب أهل الحق لهذا عبارة ثالثة ؛ لأنه لما كان فنا ثالثاً واتَمُّوا فيه بكتاب الله تعالى، فسموه كسباً، وليس مناقضاً للجبر ولا للاختيار، بل هو جامع بينهما عند من فهمه، وفعل الله تعالى يسمى اختياراً بشرط ألا يُفهم من الاختيار إرادة بعد تحير وتردد، فإن ذلك في حقه محال، وجميع الألفاظ المذكورة في اللغات لا يمكن أن تُستعمل في حق الله تعالى إلا على نوع من الاستعارة والتجوز، وذكر ذلك لا يليق بهذا العلم ويطول القول فيه).
Verily, the servant is compelled, having no choice in the occurrence of all his actions from him; he is like a feather suspended in the air, the winds sway it right and left. Their poet said, objecting to the People of the Sunnah: [al-Basīṭ] What is the servant's device while the decrees are running upon him in every state, O observer? He cast him into the sea bound and said to him: Beware, beware of getting wet with water! And some of the People of the Sunnah answered him (1) with his saying: [al-Basīṭ] If his due is gentleness, he will not be touched by wetness, and he will not care about binding and casting; and if the decree of the Master is for his drowning, then he is (already) drowned even if cast in a desert. And what must be believed is that some of his actions are issued by his choice and the others by compulsion, due to what every rational person finds of the necessary difference between the movement of striking and the movement of the trembling person. (2) Al-Kasb affirms apparent choice, and whoever denies it—and they are the Jabriyya—denies apparent choice. Al-Ujhuri. (1) His saying: (And some of the People of the Sunnah answered him etc.) And some others also answered with his saying: Allah is not asked about His actions at all, for He is the Wise in deprivation and giving; He distinguishes with bounty some people and has mercy on them, and the opposite of that is not hidden from the observer. End. His saying: (Also, and some of the People of the Sunnah answered him etc.) This answer is not apparent; rather, the apparent answer is the distinction between the one obligated (mukallaf) and the one who was cast into the sea bound and forbidden from getting wet, because the obligated one has apparent choice and kasb, unlike the bound one. End. Al-Ujhuri. - (2) For al-Ḥujja al-Ghazālī said in his Iḥyāʾ (2:554): (The meaning of his being compelled is that all of that is produced in him from other than him, not from him; and the meaning of his being choosing is that he is a locus for an will that occurs in him by compulsion after the intellect's judgment that the action is purely good and suitable, and the judgment also occurs by compulsion; thus he is compelled to choose. So the action of fire in burning, for example, is pure compulsion, and the action of Allah Most High is pure choice, and the action of man is a station between the two stations; for he is compelled to choose. So the people of truth sought a third expression for this; because it was a third type, and they completed it with the Book of Allah Most High, so they called it kasb, and it is not contradictory to compulsion nor to choice, but rather combines them for whoever understands it. And the action of Allah Most High is called choice on condition that choice is not understood as will after perplexity and hesitation, for that is impossible in His right. And all the words mentioned in the languages cannot be used in the right of Allah Most High except by way of metaphor and figurative speech. And mentioning that is not befitting this science and would lengthen the discussion.)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage refutes the extreme Jabriyya (determinists) who deny any human choice, and establishes the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb (acquisition). The author begins by quoting the poem of Jawharat al-Tawḥīd, which states that the servant is compelled like a feather in the wind. A poet from the Jabriyya objects to the People of the Sunnah with a poem: he says the servant has no device, like a person thrown bound into the sea and told not to get wet. Some of the People of the Sunnah answer with a poem: if God intends mercy, the bound person will not get wet; if He intends drowning, he drowns even on land. Then the author states the correct belief: some actions are by the servant's choice, others by compulsion, proven by the necessary rational distinction between a voluntary movement (like striking) and an involuntary one (like trembling). The commentator Al-Ujhuri adds that kasb affirms apparent choice, while the Jabriyya deny it. The footnotes provide additional responses and a long citation from Ghazālī's Iḥyāʾ explaining the middle position: man is compelled to choose, and his act is called kasb, combining compulsion and choice. Ghazālī emphasizes that God's choice is not like human choice (without hesitation).

جبر
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What imagery does the Jabriyya poet use to describe the human condition?
A) A bird in a cage
B) A feather in the wind
C) A tree planted by water
D) A stone rolling downhill

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya use poetic imagery to deny human choice, but the Sunni response reframes the issue by affirming that God's decree does not negate the reality of human actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used 'it's God's will' as an excuse to avoid repenting from a sin you committed? How does this lesson challenge that excuse?
?
When you face a trial, do you feel like that bound man thrown into the sea—helpless and without choice? How does the Sunni response change how you view your own actions within God's decree?
?
Are you living as if your choices don't matter, like the Jabriyya poet, or do you truly believe that your obedience and effort are meaningful? What would you need to change today to act on that belief?

Rational Proof of Choice and the Doctrine of Kasb
Rational proof of choice and definition of kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions as proof of human choice, and the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb as the middle path between compulsion and absolute free will.

Core Idea
Every rational person knows the difference between striking and trembling; this instinctive knowledge proves humans have genuine choice.

After disposing of the poetic argument, the author presents a straightforward rational proof: the necessary distinction between deliberate actions (like striking) and involuntary ones (like trembling). This leads to the central Ashʿarī concept of kasb, which affirms an apparent choice that is both real and acquired from God. A longer citation from Imam al-Ghazali clarifies how kasb combines compulsion and choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof
Read the Arabic statement about the necessary distinction between striking and trembling. Reflect on why this is intuitively obvious.
2
Understand the kasb statement
The text says 'al-kasb affirms apparent choice' and that the Jabriyya deny it. Kasb is the Ashʿarī term for human acquisition of actions.
Kasb is not the same as free will; it is a created capacity within the servant.
3
Study Ghazali's explanation
Read the footnote from Ghazali's Ihya. He explains that man is 'compelled to choose' and that kasb is a third category between compulsion and free will.
Ghazali emphasizes that God's choice is unlike human choice (no hesitation).
4
Reflect on the middle path
Consider how this doctrine avoids both Jabriyya (pure compulsion) and Qadariyya (absolute free will).
This is the Ashʿarī solution to the problem of free will and predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you tremble from fear and when you strike in anger, you know the difference. But do you live as if your sins are a trembling—something God caused and you cannot avoid? Or do you own them as your choice? Which belief will you carry into your next moment of weakness?
?
You claim to trust God's decree, yet you plan your day as if your choices matter. If your every move is truly acquired, not created by you—will you accept full responsibility for your actions before Him, or will you hide behind 'it was written'? Decide now, before tonight's prayer.
?
Al-Ghazali said you are 'compelled to choose.' This means you cannot blame your temper on God, nor boast of your charity as your own. Which excuse have you been clinging to—'God made me do it' or 'I earned this'? Tear it up. What will you do today to act as one who acquires, not creates?

Not All Does He Do by Choice: Negation and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Author explains the line 'Not all does he do by choice' and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic rule of negation preceding generalization and how it is used to refute the Mu'tazila's claim that the servant creates his own actions.

Core Idea
When negation precedes generalization, it can mean total negation, not just partial.

This lesson delves into a subtle grammatical point in the poem that has significant theological implications. The line 'Wa laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyara' could be misinterpreted to mean the servant creates some actions. The author explains the rule of negation and generalization and clarifies that the intended meaning is complete negation, thereby refuting the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Understand the line and its apparent meaning
Read the line 'Wa laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyara' and consider its literal translation. Notice that 'kullan' (all) is the object of 'yaf'alu' (does).
The author clarifies that 'yaf'alu' here means 'creates'.
2
Learn the rule of negation and generalization
The author states a rule: when a negation instrument (like 'laysa') precedes a generalizing instrument (like 'kull'), it indicates negation of universality (salb al-umum).
This would normally mean 'not all,' implying some.
3
See how the clue changes the meaning
The author points to the clue 'wa lam yakun mu'aththiran' (and he was not an effect) as evidence that the intended meaning is universality of negation (umum al-salb), i.e., none at all.
This is similar to the Quranic verse 'Allah does not love every conceited boaster.'
4
Recognize the theological stakes
The purpose of this verse is to explicitly refute the Mu'tazila, who claim that the servant creates his own actions, and also the Jabriyya.
The author emphasizes that explicit refutation is necessary for corrupt doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اخْتِيَارَا) أي : وليس العبد يفعل كل فعل حال كون ذلك الفعل اختياراً ، فـ (كُلَّا) مفعول لـ ( يفعل ) مقدَّمٌ عليه، و(يفعل) بمعنى يخلق، فالمعنى: ليس العبد يخلقُ كلَّ فعل من أفعاله الاختيارية، وظاهر ذلك أنه يخلق بعض أفعاله الاختياريَّةِ؛ لأن القاعدة أنه إذا تقدَّمت أداة السلب على أداة العموم أفادت سلب العموم؛ كما في قولهم : لم آخذ كل الدراهم، مع أن المراد أنه لا يخلق فعلاً أبداً. وقد يُقال : قوله : (ولم يكن مؤثّراً قرينة على المعنى المراد، والقاعدة أغلبية لا كُليَّةٌ، فالمراد هنا عموم السلب، كما في قوله تعالى: (وَاللهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُخْتَالٍ فَخُورٍ [الحديد: (۲۳)، وغرضُ المصنفِ بذلك التصريح بالرد على المعتزلة في قولهم : إنَّ العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختياريَّةً، وإِنَّما صرّح بالردّ على كُلِّ من الجبرية والمعتزلة في هذا البيت مع فهم الردّ على كلِّ منهما من البيت ،قبله كما تقدَّم التنبيه عليه؛ لأنَّ القوم لا يكتفون في مقام ردّ المذاهب الفاسدة إلا بالتصريح.
His saying: 'And not all does he do by choice' means: the servant does not do every action while that action is by choice. So 'all' is the object of 'does', placed before it, and 'does' means 'creates.' The meaning is: the servant does not create every one of his voluntary actions. The apparent meaning of that is that he creates some of his voluntary actions, because the rule is that when a negation instrument precedes a generalizing instrument, it indicates negation of universality, as in their saying: 'I did not take all the dirhams.' However, the intended meaning is that he never creates any action. And it may be said: his saying: 'and he was not an effect' is a proof for the intended meaning, and the rule is probable not categorical, so the intended meaning here is universality of negation, as in His saying: 'And Allah does not love every conceited boaster' [Q. 57:23]. And the author's purpose in that is to explicitly refute the Mu'tazila in their saying that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. He only explicitly refutes both the Jabriyya and the Mu'tazila in this verse, while the refutation of each was understood from the verse before it, as previously pointed out, because the folk are not satisfied in the context of refuting corrupt doctrines except with explicit mention.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage explains the second line of the poem: 'And not all does he do by choice' (wa laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyara). The author clarifies that 'does' here means 'creates' (yakhluq). The phrase 'not all' (laysa kullan) could be interpreted as negation of universality, implying that the servant creates some of his actions—a view that would align with the Mu'tazila. However, the author argues that the intended meaning is total negation: the servant creates no actions at all. He supports this by noting the context: the line 'and he was not an effect' (wa lam yakun mu'aththiran) serves as a clue (qarina) that the negation is universal. He also compares it to the Quranic verse 'Allah does not love every conceited boaster' (Q. 57:23), which negates love for all such people, not just some. The purpose of this verse is to explicitly refute the Mu'tazila (who claim that the servant creates his own actions) and also the Jabriyya, as the previous verse implicitly refuted both. The author stresses that in refuting corrupt doctrines, explicit mention is necessary.

Key Terms
أداة السلب
أداة العموم
سلب العموم
عموم السلب
قرينة
الرد على المعتزلة
الرد على الجبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'laysa kullan' ('not all') necessarily means 'some but not all.'
Correction: When negation precedes a generalizing word, it often indicates negation of universality, but with a contextual clue, it can indicate universality of negation (none at all).

Checkpoint
What is the author's intended meaning of 'laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyara'?
A) The servant creates some of his actions by choice
B) The servant creates none of his actions by choice
C) The servant creates all his actions by choice
D) The servant creates his actions by compulsion

Key Takeaway
The author uses a linguistic rule and a contextual clue to assert that the servant creates none of his actions, thereby refuting the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'not all does he do by choice', did you instinctively think it meant 'he does some by choice'? What does that reveal about your own tendency to carve out a space for your own will separate from Allah's?
?
The author insists on explicit refutation of false doctrines. Is there a belief about divine decree or free will that you have avoided confronting directly because it would demand a change in how you live? Will you write it down and seek the truth?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah creates every one of your actions, including your choices, how would it reshape your sense of responsibility and your reliance on Him in your next decision?

Reward is Pure Grace, Punishment is Pure Justice
Author explains the verse about grace and justice with grammatical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that reward is pure grace and punishment is pure justice, and how the Arabic 'fa' indicates this logical connection.

Core Idea
No action merits reward or punishment; all is by God's grace and justice.

In this passage, the author explains the third verse of the poem, which states a core Ash'ari principle: reward and punishment are not earned by actions but are expressions of God's grace and justice. He also clarifies the grammatical role of the 'fa' in the verse, revealing a hidden condition that ties this principle to the previous discussion on divine causality. This foundation is key to understanding later debates about human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the author immediately connects it to the earlier discussion on divine creation of actions.
The verse is: 'فإن يثبنا فبمحض الفضل وإن يعذب فبمحض العدل'.
2
Focus on the 'tafrīʿ' concept and the 'fa'
The author explains the 'fa' as either for branching (tafrīʿ) or the explicative 'fa' (fāʾ al-faṣīḥa). Understand how each interpretation reinforces the theological point.
The suppressed condition is: 'when you know God's exclusive creation, then if He rewards...'.
3
Reflect on why reward must be grace and punishment justice
Since actions do not inherently deserve anything, reward is entirely God's favor and punishment is His perfect justice. Consider how this differs from views that reward is earned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَإِنْ يُشِبْنَا ... إلخ) مفرّح على ما تقدَّم من وجوب انفراده تعالى بخلق خير أفعال العباد، وأنَّه ليس لهم فيها سوى الكسب، ووجه التفريع أنّه لم يحصل منهم يستحقون به ثواباً، ولا شر يستحقون به عقاباً، فالفاء للتفريع، ويصح أن تكون فاء الفصيحة؛ لأنَّها أفصحت عن شرط محذوف، والتقدير : إذا علمت انفراده تعالی بخلق أفعالنا خيراً كانت أو شرًا فإن يثبنا .. إلى آخره .
[الخلاف في إثابة الجن على الطاعة]
تنبيه : اتفقوا على أن بني آدم مثابون ومعاقبون.
وأما الملائكة فسيأتي الكلام في إثابتهم عند قول المصنف: (بكل عبد حافظون وكلوا).
وأما الجن فقد اتفق العلماء على أنَّ كافرهم مُعذِّبٌ في الآخرة، واختلف في مؤمنهم على أقوال، فقيل: إنَّهم كالإنس فيثابون على الطاعة، ويعاقبون على المعصية، وقيل: لا ثواب لهم إلا النجاة من النار، ثم يقال لهم : كونوا تراباً كالبهائم،
His saying: 'For if He rewards us ... etc.' is a branching off from what preceded of the obligation of His exclusivity Most High in creating the good of the servants' actions, and that they have nothing in them but acquisition. The aspect of the branching is that no good occurred from them by which they deserve reward, nor evil by which they deserve punishment. So the 'fa' is for branching, and it is possible for it to be the 'fa' of explication (fāʾ al-faṣīḥa), because it discloses a deleted condition. The estimation is: when you know His exclusivity Most High in creating our actions, whether good or evil, then if He rewards us ... to the end.
[Difference of opinion on rewarding the jinn for obedience]
A note: They agreed that the children of Adam are rewarded and punished. As for the angels, the discussion of their reward will come at the author's saying: 'With every servant, guardians and trustees.' As for the jinn, the scholars agreed that their disbeliever is punished in the Hereafter, and they differed regarding their believer on several opinions. It is said: They are like humans, so they are rewarded for obedience and punished for disobedience. And it is said: They have no reward except salvation from the Fire; then it is said to them: Be dust like the beasts.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage explains the third verse of the poem: 'For if He rewards us, it is by pure grace; and if He punishes, it is by pure justice.' The author notes that this verse branches off (tafrīʿ) from the previous discussion about God's exclusive creation of actions and man's mere acquisition (kasb). Since no action from the servant merits reward or punishment, reward is pure grace (faḍl) and punishment is pure justice (ʿadl). The 'fa' (then) is either for branching or is the 'fāʾ al-faṣīḥa' (the explicative fa) that reveals a suppressed condition: 'When you know God's exclusive creation, then if He rewards...' Then the author adds a note (tanbīh) about the reward of jinn. He states the consensus that the believing jinn are not punished, but there is disagreement on whether they are rewarded like humans or merely saved from Hell and then turned to dust like animals.

Key Terms
إثابة
فضل
عدل
تفريع
فاء الفصيحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human actions merit reward or punishment by themselves.
Correction: The passage states that no good or evil from the servant deserves reward or punishment; all is by God's grace (reward) and justice (punishment).

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the 'fa' in 'فإن يثبنا' indicate?
A) It indicates a condition
B) It indicates branching or explication
C) It indicates causation
D) It indicates surprise

Key Takeaway
Reward is pure grace and punishment is pure justice because actions do not inherently deserve anything.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your good deeds put God in your debt? How does this lesson's claim that reward is pure grace challenge that sense of entitlement? What would change in your worship if you truly believed you deserve nothing?
?
When you experience suffering or hardship, do you interpret it as punishment you 'deserve'? How does understanding that punishment is pure justice (not necessarily tied to your specific actions) reshape your relationship with God's decree? Will you submit to His justice or resent it?
?
The verse says 'if He rewards us'—implying it is not automatic. Does your daily prayer feel like a request for grace, or a demand for payment? What step will you take today to approach God with the humility that expects only grace?

The Reward of Jinn
Author discusses the consensus and disagreement on jinn's reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus and disagreement among scholars regarding the reward and punishment of jinn in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Jinn are rewarded and punished, but scholars differ on the nature of their reward.

After establishing the principle of reward and punishment for humans, the author turns to a specific case: the jinn. While there is agreement that believing jinn are not punished in Hell, there is a notable disagreement on whether they receive reward like humans or are simply saved from Fire and turned to dust. This discussion highlights how theological principles apply to different creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the note on consensus for humans and deferred angels
Start by reading the full passage, noticing the structure: first humans, then angels (deferred), then jinn.
The author uses 'تنبيه' (note) to introduce this secondary topic.
2
Focus on the two opinions about jinn reward
There are two main opinions: (1) jinn are like humans, rewarded and punished; (2) jinn are only saved from Hell and then become dust. Compare the two.
The second opinion reduces the reward to mere salvation.
3
Reflect on the implication of the opinion granting no reward but salvation
Consider what this says about the status of jinn in Islamic theology—they are not obligated in the same way as humans, perhaps.
This is a theological point about the nature of jinn.
