God's Actions Are Not Necessitated
Narrative followed by theological argument about God's freedom from obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, in Ash'ari theology, obedience does not obligate reward nor disobedience punishment, and that God is absolutely free and not questioned about His actions.

Core Idea
God is absolutely free; no act of obedience or disobedience creates a claim upon Him.

This lesson begins with a poignant story of a saint troubled by the suffering of innocents in Baghdad. Through a dream, he receives a profound theological principle: God is not constrained by human notions of deserving reward or punishment. We will unpack this principle and see why, for Ash'aris, obedience and disobedience are merely 'signs' of the usual outcome, not causes of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and its question
Read the story of Shaykh ʿAfīf al-Dīn al-Zāhid. Notice the question he asks God about the suffering of children. This sets up the theological puzzle.
The story is a historical narrative but carries a deep theological lesson.
2
Find the answer in the dream poem
The dream poem instructs the shaykh not to object and asserts that God is not questioned about His actions. Reflect on the meaning of the poetic lines.
The poem is a key transition from story to doctrine.
3
Understand the doctrine of signs
The passage then states that obedience does not necessitate reward, nor disobedience punishment; they are only signs (amārāt). Even if God reversed the sign, it would be good. Consider the implications for God's freedom.
This is the core Ash'ari teaching: God is not bound by any external necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكي عن الشيخ عفيف الدين الزاهد أنَّه كان بمصر، فبلغه ما وقع ببغداد من القتل، فإنَّه وقع السيف فيها أربعين يوماً فقُتل ألف ألف، وعَلَّقت النصارى المصاحف في أعناق الكلاب، وجعلوا المساجد كنائس، وألقوا كتب الأئمة في الدجلة، حتى صارت كالجسر يمرُّ الخيلُ عليها ، فأنكر الشيخ عفيف الدين ذلك، وقال: يا رَبِّ ؛ كيف هذا وفيهم الأطفال، ومن لا ذنب له؟ فرأى في النوم رجلاً ومعه كتاب فأخذه فإذا فيه : [من المتقارب] دَعِ الإِعْتِرَاضَ فَمَا الْأَمْرُ لَكْ وَلَا الحُكْمُ فِي حَرَكَاتِ الفَلَكْ وَلَا تَسْأَلِ الله عَنْ فِعْلِهِ فَمَنْ خَاصَ لُجَّةَ بَحْرِ هَلَكْ وبالجملة فهو سبحانه وتعالى لا تنفعه طاعةٌ ولا تضره معصية، والكل بخلقه، فليست الطاعة (۱) مستلزمة للثواب وليست المعصية مستلزمة للعقاب، وإنَّما هما أمارتان تدلان على الثواب لمن أطاع والعقاب لمن عصى، حتى لو عكس (۲) دلالتهما بأنْ قال : مَنْ أطاعني عذبته ومَنْ عصاني أثبته لكان ذلك منه حسناً، فلا حرج عليه، لا يُسأل عما يفعل. وهذا كله سب العقل (٣) ، وأما بحسب (1) قوله: فليست الطاعة... إلخ) أي: إن الطاعة لا توجب على الله إثابة وكذا المعصية لا توجب على الله عقاباً، وهذا هو عين ما في المتن. انتهى أجهوري. (۲) قوله: (حتى لو عكس دلالتهما ... إلخ) هذا تفريع على قوله : (فليست الطاعة مستلزمة للثواب ... إلخ)، وقوله : (إنما هما أمارتان ... إلخ) جملة معترضة بين التفريع والمفرع عليه ثم المتبادر من عكس الدلالة أن الله تعالى أن يرجع عن وعد المطيع بالثواب إلى وعده بالعذاب وأن له أن يرجع عن وعد العاصي بالعقاب إلى وعده بالثواب، وهذا مع كونه غير ظاهر في نفسه، لا يظهر تفريعه على ما تقدم من أن الطاعة لا تستلزم الثواب والمعصية لا تستلزم العقاب، بل الظاهر أن يقال بدله : حتى لو عذب الله المطيع وأثاب العاصي لكان حسناً، فإن كان هذا مراده بعكس الدلالة كان التفريع ظاهراً . انتهى أجهوري. + (٣) قوله : (وهذا إلخ) الإشارة إلى ما قدمه من أن الطاعة لا تستلزم الثواب والمعصية لا تستلزم العقاب، مع ما فرعه عليه بقوله : حتى لو عكس دلالتهما بناء على أن المراد بعكس الدلالة أن الله تعالى أن يُعذب المطيع، وله أن يثيب العاصي. وأما قوله فيما تقدم : ( و إنما هما أمارتان ... إلخ) فليس بالعقل، بل هو أمر شرعي كما لا يخفى، ولو أسقطه وأوصل التفريع بالمفرع عليه لكان أظهر، والمحشي تابع في هذا كله للشيخ عبد السلام، هذا ما ظهر بعد التأمل. انتهى أجهوري.
It is narrated about Shaykh ʿAfīf al-Dīn al-Zāhid that he was in Egypt, and news reached him of what happened in Baghdad of killing; for the sword had fallen among them for forty days and a million were killed. The Christians hung copies of the Quran around the necks of dogs, turned mosques into churches, and threw the books of the imams into the Tigris until it became like a bridge over which horses passed. Shaykh ʿAfīf al-Dīn disapproved of that and said: 'O Lord, how is this when among them are children and those who have no sin?' So he saw in a dream a man with a book; he took it and found in it: [al-Mutaqārib] 'Abandon objection, for the matter is not yours, nor is the judgment concerning the movements of the sphere. And do not ask God about His act, for whoever dives into the deep of the sea perishes.' In sum, He is exalted and high: obedience does not benefit Him nor does disobedience harm Him. All is by His creation. So obedience (1) does not necessitate reward, nor does disobedience necessitate punishment. Rather, they are two signs that indicate reward for whoever obeys and punishment for whoever disobeys. Even if He reversed (2) their indication by saying: 'Whoever obeys Me, I will punish him, and whoever disobeys Me, I will reward him,' that would be good from Him. So there is no constraint upon Him; He is not asked about what He does. And all of this is a matter of reason (3)… But according to (1) His saying: 'So obedience does not necessitate... etc.' meaning: obedience does not obligate reward upon God, and likewise disobedience does not obligate punishment upon God. This is exactly what is in the text. End of al-Ajhuri. (2) His saying: 'Even if He reversed their indication... etc.' This is a branching off from his saying: 'Obedience does not necessitate reward... etc.' And his saying: 'Rather they are two signs... etc.' is an intervening sentence between the branch and the original. Then the apparent meaning of 'reverse of indication' is that God may go back from His promise of reward for the obedient to promising punishment, and may go back from His promise of punishment for the disobedient to promising reward. This, however, is not apparent in itself, and its branching off from what preceded – that obedience does not necessitate reward and disobedience does not necessitate punishment – is not clear. Instead, it is apparent that one should say instead: 'Even if God punished the obedient and rewarded the disobedient, that would be good.' If this is what he meant by 'reverse of indication', then the derivation is clear. End of al-Ajhuri. + (3) His saying: 'And this... etc.' The reference is to what he previously presented: that obedience does not necessitate reward and disobedience does not necessitate punishment, along with what he branched off from it in his saying: 'Even if He reversed their indication', based on the understanding that by 'reverse of indication' is meant that God may punish the obedient and may reward the disobedient. As for his earlier saying: 'Rather they are two signs... etc.', it is not by reason, but rather it is a matter of revelation, as is not hidden. If he had dropped it and connected the branch directly to the original, it would have been clearer. The commentator follows in all this Shaykh ʿAbd al-Salām. This is what appeared after reflection. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a narrative followed by a theological argument. It begins with a story about Shaykh ʿAfīf al-Dīn who questioned God's justice regarding the suffering of innocents in Baghdad. He receives a dream with a poem telling him not to object. The commentary then draws the doctrinal point: God is not benefited by obedience nor harmed by disobedience; all actions are created by Him. Obedience does not necessitate reward, nor disobedience punishment; they are only signs (amārāt) indicating the usual outcome. Even if God reversed the sign—promising punishment for obedience and reward for disobedience—that would be good and He would not be questioned. The marginal notes clarify that this point is not purely rational but based on revelation, and explain that the reversal of indication means God's freedom to do contrary to His usual promise, not a change in His promise itself. The commentator (al-Ajhuri) notes that the intervening phrase 'they are two signs' is a parenthetical that could be omitted for clarity.

Key Terms
أمارتان
عكس الدلالة
لا يُسأل عمّا يفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to reward obedience and punish disobedience because He is just.
Correction: Obedience and disobedience are only signs, not causes of obligation; God is free to act otherwise and is not questioned.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between obedience and reward?
A) Obedience necessitates reward upon God.
B) Obedience is a sign that usually indicates reward, but not a cause of obligation.
C) Obedience causes God to be pleased, making reward necessary.
D) Obedience and reward are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
God is absolutely free; no act of obedience or disobedience imposes an obligation on Him, and He is not questioned about His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Story of Iblis and al-Shafi'i on Divine Justice
Anecdote of Iblis questioning al-Shafi'i about divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response to the problem of predestination and justice as illustrated by the anecdote of Iblis and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
God's sovereignty transcends human categories of justice.

This lesson uses a famous anecdote to clarify a subtle point about divine justice and human free will. The story of Iblis questioning Imam al-Shafi'i distills the Sunni position on qadar. We will read the exchange, unpack its logic, and see why it refutes a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the setting: Iblis appears before al-Shafi'i and poses a question about God's justice.
Pay attention to the wording of Iblis's question.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Identify the trap Iblis sets: if God created him for His own purpose and then judges him, is that just or unjust? Notice how al-Shafi'i reframes the question.
Al-Shafi'i distinguishes between God's will and the creature's desire.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the implications: al-Shafi'i's answer affirms God's absolute sovereignty and that He is not bound by human conceptions of justice.
The story warns against using limited human reasoning to judge God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُحكى أنَّ إبليس لعنه الله تمثل بين يدي الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، وقال: يا إمام؛ ما تقولُ فيمن خلقني لما اختار واستعملني فيما اختار، وبعد ذلك إن شاء أدخلني الجنَّة، وإن شاء أدخلني النَّارِ، أَعَدَلَ في ذلك أم جَارَ؟ قال الإمام: فنظرتُ في مسألته فألهمني الله تعالى أن قلت: يا هذا؛ إن كان خلقك لما تريد أنت فقد ظلمك وإن خلقك لما يريد هو فلا يُسأل عما يفعلُ وهم يسألون، فاضمحلَّ إبليس وتلاشى، ثم قال والله يا شافعي؛ لقد أخرجت بمسألتي هذه سبعين ألف عابد من ديوان العبودية إلى ديوان الزندقة.
And it is narrated that Iblis, may Allah curse him, appeared before al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, and said: O Imam, what do you say about the one who created me for what He chose and employed me in what He chose, then after that if He wills He enters me into Paradise, and if He wills He enters me into the Fire; is that just or unjust? The Imam said: I reflected on his question and Allah inspired me to say: O this one, if He created you for what you yourself want, then He has wronged you; but if He created you for what He wills, then He is not questioned about what He does, while they will be questioned. So Iblis dissolved and vanished, then he said: By Allah, O Shafi'i, you have with this question of mine removed seventy thousand worshipers from the register of servitude to the register of heresy.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote illustrating the Sunni response to the problem of predestination and divine justice. Iblis poses a dilemma: if God created him for what He chose and then judges him, is that just or unjust? Al-Shafi'i, divinely inspired, responds with a distinction: if God created Iblis for Iblis's own desire, then it would be unjust; but if God created him for His own will, then God is not questioned. This answer silences Iblis, who admits it undermines the faith of many devotees.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's reply shows that God's actions are not subject to human standards of justice because He is the ultimate source of all existence and will.

Understanding 'Good in Your Hands, Evil Not to You'
Explains the hadith and refutes Mu'tazila implication regarding divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of the hadith 'Good is in Your hands, evil is not to You' and how it aligns with the Sunni position that God wills all things, including evil.

Core Idea
Evil is not attributed to God as a good act, but all occurs by His will.

This lesson tackles a common objection: does the hadith 'Good is in Your hands, evil is not to You' mean God does not create evil? The passage clarifies the hadith's intent and shows the Mu'tazila implication would undermine divine sovereignty. By understanding this, you will see how Ahl al-Sunna harmonize the hadith with comprehensive divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the objection
Read the Arabic hadith and the Mu'tazila implication. Understand the potential challenge to Sunni belief.
The objection arises because evil is mentioned in the hadith.
2
Examine the Sunni explanation
Notice how the author explains the hadith: good is by God's power, and evil is not a means of drawing near to Him.
This preserves God's will over all things.
3
Consider the Mu'tazila consequence
The passage shows that if evil is not willed by God, then most events in His kingdom would be outside His will, which is absurd.
Evils are more numerous than goods in worldly experience.
4
See the counter-proof
The hadith 'What Allah wills comes to be' refutes the Mu'tazila position, affirming comprehensive divine will.
This is a direct textual evidence.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Understand why the Sunni interpretation is consistent, and the Mu'tazila position leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يرد على مذهب أهل السنة حديث : الخيرُ بيديكَ، والشرُّ ليسَ إِلَيْكَ» (١) ، لأن معناه الخير بقدرتك وإرادتك، والشرُّ لا يُتقرَّبُ به إليك. ويلزم على ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة : أنَّ أكثر ما يقع في ملكه تعالى غيرُ مرادٍ له ؛ لأنَّ الشرور أكثر من الخيرات ویرده قوله : «ما شاءَ اللهُ كانَ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن».
And the hadith 'Good is in Your hands, and evil is not to You' (1) does not refute the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna, because its meaning is: good is by Your power and will, and evil is not a means of drawing near to You. And it follows from what the Mu'tazila hold that most of what occurs in His kingdom is not willed by Him, because evils are more than goods, and it is refuted by His saying: 'What Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be' (2).
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: the hadith 'Good is in Your hands, evil is not to You' seems to contradict the Sunni view that God creates evil. The author explains that the hadith means that evil is not a means of worshipful approach to God, not that He does not will or create it. Then the author notes that the Mu'tazilite position leads to the unacceptable conclusion that most events in God's kingdom are not willed by Him, which contradicts the clear hadith 'What Allah wills comes to be...'.

Key Terms
الخير
الشر
المشيئة
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Good is in Your hands, evil is not to You' means God does not create or will evil.
Correction: It means evil is not a means of drawing near to God, but all events, including evil, occur by His will.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'evil is not to You' in the hadith according to Ahl al-Sunna?
A) Evil is not created by God.
B) Evil is not a means of drawing near to God.
C) Evil does not exist.
D) God does not will evil.

Key Takeaway
The hadith does not deny God's will over evil; rather, it clarifies that evil is not a way to approach Him, and all things occur by His decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience evil or hardship, do you truly believe that it is only by Allah's will? What prevents you from submitting to this reality?
?
The hadith teaches that evil is not a means to draw near to Allah. Examine your own life: are there any sinful actions you are using to achieve a 'good' purpose? How does this hadith challenge that?
?
The Mu'tazila erred by making their logic override clear texts. Where in your own understanding of Allah's decree are you tempted to rely on your own reasoning over revelation? What would it take to surrender that area?

Two Types of Qadariyya and Obligation of Belief in Qada and Qadar
Refutation of Qadariyya sects and exposition of two types of heretical predestinarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct sects of Qadariyya, their key errors, and why both are refuted by the obligation to believe in divine predestination.

Core Idea
There are two Qadariyya groups — one denies God's eternal knowledge, the other denies His creation of actions.

This passage from the commentary on al-Jawhara clarifies an important theological distinction. The author first establishes the obligation to believe in qada and qadar, then identifies two groups that deviate from this belief. Understanding these two types is essential for recognizing how heretical views on predestination manifest differently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its translation. Note the grammatical structure of the opening line as it introduces the topic.
The passage begins with a grammatical note before launching into the refutation.
2
Identify the two types
Find where the author mentions 'فهما قدريتان' and list the characteristics of each group. Notice the first group denies God's prior knowledge; the second affirms it but attributes actions to human power.
The first group is extinct; the second is still addressed.
3
Understand the naming justification
The author defends calling both groups Qadariyya because they both excessively involve themselves in discussing qadar, albeit in opposite ways.
This prevents confusion about the term's application.
4
Link to previous refutation
Notice how the second group was already refuted by the line 'فخالق لعبده وما عمل' (For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does). This ties back to earlier material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضاء والقدر] ٥٤ - وَوَاجِبٌ إِيمَانُنَا بِالقَدَرِ وَبِالقَضَا كَمَا أَنَى فِي الخَـ قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ إِيمَانُنا ... إلخ) (۲) : (واجب) خبر مقدَّم. و(إيماننا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، وغرضُ المصنف بذلك الردُّ على القَدَريَّةِ التي تنفي القدر تعالى لم يُقدر الأمور أزلاً، وتقول : الأمرُ أنْفٌ؛ أي: يستأنفه الله عِلْماً وتزعم أنَّه حال وقوعه، ولقبوا بالقَدَريَّة لخوضهم في القَدَر ، حيث بالغوا في نفيه، ولا يُقال: مُثبت القَدَر أحقُّ أن يُنسب إليه ؛ لأنا نقول : كما يصح نسبةُ مثبته إليه يصح نسبةُ نافيه إليه إذا بالغ في نفيه، وهؤلاء انقرضوا قبل الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، وأما القَدَريَّة التي تنسب أفعال العبيد إلى قَدَرِهم مع كونهم مطبقين على أنه تعالى عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها، فقد تقدَّم الردُّ عليهم بقوله سابقاً : (فخالق لعبده وما عمل). فهما قدريتان: أولى : وهي تنكرُ سَبْقَ علمه تعالى بالأشياء قبل وقوعها، وتخوض في القدر، حيث بالغت في نفيه . وثانية : وهي تنسب أفعال العباد إلى قَدَرِهم ومذهب هذه وإن كان مذهباً باطلاً أخف من مذهب الفرقة الأولى ؛ فإنه كفر.
[Section on Predestination] 54 - And obligatory is our faith in al-qadar and al-qada as has come in ... His saying: (And obligatory is our faith ... etc.) (2): (obligatory) is a forward predicate, and (our faith) is a delayed subject. The author's purpose in that is to refute the Qadariyya who deny qadar, claiming that Allah did not pre-ordain things from eternity, and say: The matter is fresh (anf), i.e., Allah originates it in knowledge and claim that it is at the time of its occurrence. They were called Qadariyya because of their excessive involvement in qadar (predestination) in denying it. And it cannot be said that the establisher of qadar is more deserving of being attributed to it, for we say: just as the attributions of the established of qadar to him are correct, so too the denier of qadar can be attributed to him if he goes to extremes in denying it. These died out before Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him. As for the Qadariyya who attribute the actions of servants to their own qadar (power) while agreeing that Allah knows actions before they occur, the refutation of them has preceded in his earlier statement: 'For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does.' So there are two Qadariyya: The first: which denies the precedence of His knowledge of things before their occurrence, and delves into qadar, exaggerating in its denial. The second: which attributes the actions of servants to their qadar (power), and the madhhab of this latter, though it is a false madhhab, is lighter than the madhhab of the first group; for it is disbelief.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a verse from the poem about the obligation to believe in qada and qadar. The author explains the grammatical structure and then identifies the purpose: to refute the Qadariyya. He distinguishes two groups of Qadariyya. The first group denies that God predetermined things from eternity, claiming that God's knowledge is created at the time of event (a view that is now extinct). The second group acknowledges God's prior knowledge but attributes human actions to the servant's own power ('qadar' here meaning ability). The author notes that the first group is more extreme and is guilty of kufr (disbelief), while the second group's error is lighter but still false. The earlier line 'Fakhaliqu li'abdihi wa ma 'amal' refutes the second group by affirming that God creates both the servant and his actions. The author also defends the naming: both groups are called Qadariyya because they are excessively involved in discussing qadar, even if in opposite ways.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
القدرية
الأمر أنف
علم الله
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the term Qadariyya only refers to those who deny predestination entirely.
Correction: The passage shows there is a second group that affirms predestination but attributes actions to human agency, also called Qadariyya due to excessive involvement in the topic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a characteristic of the second group of Qadariyya?
A) They deny God's knowledge of future events.
B) They claim humans create their own actions.
C) They believe God creates actions but humans acquire them.
D) They deny the existence of qadar entirely.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to believe in qada and qadar refutes two heretical groups: one denies God's eternal knowledge, and the other denies that God creates all actions.

Obligation of Faith in Predestination and Refutation of the First Qadariyya
Commentary on the obligation of faith in qadr and refutation of the first Qadariyya sect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of believing in qadr and how the first Qadariyya sect is refuted for denying God's eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Faith in predestination is obligatory; the Qadariyya are refuted for denying God's prior knowledge.

The commentator begins by explaining a line of poetry that declares faith in qadr obligatory. This leads to a refutation of those who deny that God decrees matters from eternity. In this lesson, we will examine the first group of Qadariyya and the linguistic reason for their name.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the portion of the text that covers the obligation statement and the refutation of the first Qadariyya. Note the grammatical structure.
2
Understand the error of the first Qadariyya
The first group denies that God had knowledge of events before they occur. They say 'the matter is fresh.' Identify how this contradicts obligatory faith.
This group became extinct before Imam al-Shafi'i.
3
Reflect on the naming of Qadariyya
The commentary explains that both affirmers and deniers of qadr can be called Qadariyya if they exaggerate. Consider why the name is applied to deniers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ إِيمَانُنا ... إلخ) : (واجب) خبر مقدَّم. و(إيماننا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، وغرضُ المصنف بذلك الردُّ على القَدَريَّةِ التي تنفي القدر تعالى لم يُقدر الأمور أزلاً، وتقول : الأمرُ أنْفٌ؛ أي: يستأنفه الله عِلْماً وتزعم أنَّه حال وقوعه، ولقبوا بالقَدَريَّة لخوضهم في القَدَر ، حيث بالغوا في نفيه، ولا يُقال: مُثبت القَدَر أحقُّ أن يُنسب إليه ؛ لأنا نقول : كما يصح نسبةُ مثبته إليه يصح نسبةُ نافيه إليه إذا بالغ في نفيه، وهؤلاء انقرضوا قبل الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، وأما القَدَريَّة التي تنسب أفعال العبيد إلى قَدَرِهم مع كونهم مطبقين على أنه تعالى عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها، فقد تقدَّم الردُّ عليهم بقوله سابقاً : (فخالق لعبده وما عمل). فهما قدريتان: أولى : وهي تنكرُ سَبْقَ علمه تعالى بالأشياء قبل وقوعها، وتخوض في القدر، حيث بالغت في نفيه . وثانية : وهي تنسب أفعال العباد إلى قَدَرِهم ومذهب هذه وإن كان مذهباً باطلاً أخف من مذهب الفرقة الأولى ؛ فإنه كفر.
His statement: 'And obligatory is our faith in the Decree and the Predestination... etc.': ('Obligatory') is a predicate preceded, and ('our faith') is a subject delayed. The author's purpose in that is to refute the Qadariyya who deny the Predestination (al-qadr) – exalted is He – [saying] He did not decree matters from eternity, and they say: 'The matter is fresh' (al-amru anfun), meaning God originates knowledge of it at the time of its occurrence, and they claim that He [knows it] at the moment of its happening. They were called Qadariyya due to their immersion in discourse about predestination (al-qadr), where they exaggerated in denying it. And it is not said: 'The affirmer of predestination is more deserving of being attributed to it,' because we say: just as the attribution of its affirmer to it is valid, so is the attribution of its denier to it valid if he exaggerates in denying it. These people became extinct before Imam al-Shafi'i, may God be pleased with him. As for the Qadariyya who attribute the actions of servants to their own power (qadarihim) while they agree that He – exalted is He – knows the actions of servants before their occurrence, their refutation has already preceded by his earlier statement: 'For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does.' Thus they are two Qadariyya: the first: they deny the precedence of His knowledge – exalted is He – of things before their occurrence, and they immerse in predestination, exaggerating in its denial. The second: they attribute the actions of servants to their own power. The school of this second group, although it is a false school lighter than the school of the first group, is nevertheless disbelief (kufr).
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry that states the obligation of faith in al-qadr (predestination) and al-qada (decree). The commentator first explains the grammatical structure: 'واجب' (obligatory) is a predicate and 'إيماننا' (our faith) is a subject. The author's purpose is to refute the Qadariyya sect, who deny that God predetermined matters from eternity. They say 'الأمر أنف' meaning that God creates knowledge of events at the time they occur, not before. They are called Qadariyya because of their excessive involvement in discussing qadr, going to extremes in denying it. The commentator then refutes the idea that the affirmers of qadr are more deserving of the name; rather, both affirmers and deniers can be called Qadariyya if they exaggerate. The first group (who deny pre-knowledge) became extinct before Imam al-Shafi'i. The second group, who attribute actions to their own power while affirming God's knowledge, were already refuted earlier by the line 'فخالق لعبده وما عمل' (For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does). The commentator then summarizes the two groups: the first denies God's prior knowledge; the second attributes acts to human capability. The second group's doctrine, though less severe, is still kufr.

القضاء
القدر
القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name Qadariyya is only appropriate for those who affirm predestination, not those who deny it.
Correction: As explained, both affirmers and deniers can be called Qadariyya if they exaggerate in their discourse about qadr.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first Qadariyya deny God's eternal knowledge, and they are called Qadariyya because of their extreme denial of qadr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in qadr, but do you ever make decisions as if Allah did not already know the outcome? Think of a recent choice—did you act out of fear or anxiety as if the future was uncertain to your Lord? What would it mean to fully trust in His eternal knowledge?
?
The Qadariyya were uncomfortable with the idea that Allah knows all before it happens, because it seemed to threaten human free will. Is there a part of your life where you resent Allah's control? Honestly, do you ever wish you had autonomy that doesn't fit under His decree? How does that reveal a lack of submission?
?
The first Qadariyya became extinct, but their mindset—denying divine knowledge to preserve human freedom—still surfaces. Have you ever caught yourself thinking, 'Allah doesn't know this yet because I haven't decided'? That thought is a seed of the same error. Will you uproot it today by affirming that Allah knew you before you were born?

The Second Qadariyya: Attributing Actions to Human Power
Refutation of the second Qadariyya attributing actions to human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Qadariyya sect that attributes actions to human power and why their belief is considered disbelief (kufr).

Core Idea
Attributing actions to your own power while affirming God's knowledge is still kufr.

The previous lesson covered the Qadariyya who deny God's knowledge. Now we turn to a second group who affirm God's knowledge but attribute actions to their own power. This lesson explains why this view is also false and constitutes disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the relevant passage
Read the part of the text that introduces the second Qadariyya and the refutation from the earlier line 'فخالق لعبده وما عمل'.
The commentator mentions that this refutation was already given.

The earlier line establishes that Allah is the Creator of the servant and his actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ إِيمَانُنا ... إلخ) : (واجب) خبر مقدَّم. و(إيماننا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، وغرضُ المصنف بذلك الردُّ على القَدَريَّةِ التي تنفي القدر تعالى لم يُقدر الأمور أزلاً، وتقول : الأمرُ أنْفٌ؛ أي: يستأنفه الله عِلْماً وتزعم أنَّه حال وقوعه، ولقبوا بالقَدَريَّة لخوضهم في القَدَر ، حيث بالغوا في نفيه، ولا يُقال: مُثبت القَدَر أحقُّ أن يُنسب إليه ؛ لأنا نقول : كما يصح نسبةُ مثبته إليه يصح نسبةُ نافيه إليه إذا بالغ في نفيه، وهؤلاء انقرضوا قبل الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، وأما القَدَريَّة التي تنسب أفعال العبيد إلى قَدَرِهم مع كونهم مطبقين على أنه تعالى عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها، فقد تقدَّم الردُّ عليهم بقوله سابقاً : (فخالق لعبده وما عمل). فهما قدريتان: أولى : وهي تنكرُ سَبْقَ علمه تعالى بالأشياء قبل وقوعها، وتخوض في القدر، حيث بالغت في نفيه . وثانية : وهي تنسب أفعال العباد إلى قَدَرِهم ومذهب هذه وإن كان مذهباً باطلاً أخف من مذهب الفرقة الأولى ؛ فإنه كفر.
His statement: 'And obligatory is our faith in the Decree and the Predestination... etc.': ('Obligatory') is a predicate preceded, and ('our faith') is a subject delayed. The author's purpose in that is to refute the Qadariyya who deny the Predestination (al-qadr) – exalted is He – [saying] He did not decree matters from eternity, and they say: 'The matter is fresh' (al-amru anfun), meaning God originates knowledge of it at the time of its occurrence, and they claim that He [knows it] at the moment of its happening. They were called Qadariyya due to their immersion in discourse about predestination (al-qadr), where they exaggerated in denying it. And it is not said: 'The affirmer of predestination is more deserving of being attributed to it,' because we say: just as the attribution of its affirmer to it is valid, so is the attribution of its denier to it valid if he exaggerates in denying it. These people became extinct before Imam al-Shafi'i, may God be pleased with him. As for the Qadariyya who attribute the actions of servants to their own power (qadarihim) while they agree that He – exalted is He – knows the actions of servants before their occurrence, their refutation has already preceded by his earlier statement: 'For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does.' Thus they are two Qadariyya: the first: they deny the precedence of His knowledge – exalted is He – of things before their occurrence, and they immerse in predestination, exaggerating in its denial. The second: they attribute the actions of servants to their own power. The school of this second group, although it is a false school lighter than the school of the first group, is nevertheless disbelief (kufr).
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry that states the obligation of faith in al-qadr (predestination) and al-qada (decree). The commentator first explains the grammatical structure: 'واجب' (obligatory) is a predicate and 'إيماننا' (our faith) is a subject. The author's purpose is to refute the Qadariyya sect, who deny that God predetermined matters from eternity. They say 'الأمر أنف' meaning that God creates knowledge of events at the time they occur, not before. They are called Qadariyya because of their excessive involvement in discussing qadr, going to extremes in denying it. The commentator then refutes the idea that the affirmers of qadr are more deserving of the name; rather, both affirmers and deniers can be called Qadariyya if they exaggerate. The first group (who deny pre-knowledge) became extinct before Imam al-Shafi'i. The second group, who attribute actions to their own power while affirming God's knowledge, were already refuted earlier by the line 'فخالق لعبده وما عمل' (For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does). The commentator then summarizes the two groups: the first denies God's prior knowledge; the second attributes acts to human capability. The second group's doctrine, though less severe, is still kufr.

Key Terms
القدرية
الرد
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second Qadariyya are not considered disbelievers because they affirm God's knowledge.
Correction: The passage explicitly says their school is kufr, even though it is lighter.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the second Qadariyya according to this passage?
A) They are not blameworthy.
B) Their belief is false but not kufr.
C) Their belief is kufr.
D) They are considered Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The second Qadariyya, though less extreme, is still kufr because it denies that Allah is the sole Creator of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent success you achieved. In that moment, did you feel it was from your own ability? How does the declaration that attributing actions to your own power is kufr change how you view that achievement? What will you do to shift your attribution to Allah alone?
?
The passage teaches that even while affirming Allah's knowledge, attributing actions to human power is disbelief. Is there any area of your life—your career, relationships, or spirituality—where you still feel you are the independent actor? Pray right now for Allah to grant you the tawfeeq to truly believe He is the Creator of all your actions.

Divine Decree

Being Pleased with Divine Decree
Resolves objection about being pleased with qadr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconcile the obligation to be pleased with qadr with the prohibition of being pleased with disbelief and sin.

Core Idea
Being pleased with qadr does not mean approving of sin.

This lesson addresses a critical objection: if we must be pleased with God's decree, does that require us to be pleased with disbelief and sin? The resolution lies in distinguishing between the decree itself and the decreed, and between God's creative act and the servant's acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage twice—once for overall meaning, once for detail. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow: objection, answer, critique, final resolution.
Note the technical terms: القضاء, القدر, المقضي, المقدور, الاكتساب.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that being pleased with qadr would require being pleased with disbelief and sin, which is itself sinful. Understand why this is a serious challenge.
3
Examine al-Sa'd's answer and its critique
Al-Sa'd distinguishes between qadr/qada (decree) and maqdi/muqaddar (decreed). The critique shows this distinction is problematic because being pleased with the decree implies being pleased with the decreed.
Consider why the critique is compelling.
4
Learn al-Khayali's two-aspect solution
Al-Khayali resolves the problem by noting that disbelief and sins have two aspects: as decreed by God and as acquired by the servant. We are pleased with the first aspect but not the second.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإيمان بالقضاء والقدر يستدعي الرضا بهما، فيجب الرضا بالقضاء والقدر، واستُشكل بأنَّه يلزم على ذلك الرضا بالكفر والمعاصي؛ لأنَّ الله قضى بهما وقدرهما على الشخص، مع أن الرضا بالكفر كفر، وبالمعاصي معصية. وأجيب بما قاله السعد من أنَّ الكفر والمعاصي مقضي ومُقدَّرٌ لا قضاءٌ وقَدَر، والواجب الرضا به إنَّما هو القضاء والقدر لا المقضي والمقدور، وفيه أنه لا معنى للرضا بالقضاء والقدر إلا الرضا بالمقضي والمقدور، والذي حققه الخيالي في حاشيته : أنَّ الكفر والمعاصي لهما جهتان : جهة كونهما مَقْضِيَيْنِ ومُقَدَّرَيْنِ الله . وجهه: كونهما مُكْتَسَبَيْنِ للعبد، فيجب الرضا بهما من الجهة الأولى دون الثانية . واعلم أنه وإن وجب الإيمان بالقدر لكن لا يجوز الاحتجاج به قبل الوقوع توصلاً إليه بأن قال شخص : قدَّر الله عليَّ الزنا، مثلاً ؛ وغرضه بذلك التوصل إلى الوقوع في الزنا، أو بعد الوقوع تخلصاً من الحد أو نحوه، بأن وقع شخص في الزنا مثلاً، وقال : قَدَّر الله عليَّ ذلك، وغرضه به التخلص من الحد، وأما الاحتجاج به بعد الوقوع لدفع اللّومِ عنه فقط فلا بأس به، ففي الحديث الصحيح: «إنَّ روح آدم التقث مع روح موسى عليهما الصلاة والسلام، فقال موسى لآدم: أنت أبو البشر الذي كنتَ سبباً لإخراج أولادِكَ من الجنَّةِ بأكلك من الشجرة، فقال آدم: يا موسى؛ فأنت الذي
And faith in al-qada and al-qadr necessitates being pleased with them both, so being pleased with al-qada and al-qadr is obligatory. An objection was raised: that entails being pleased with disbelief and sins, because God decreed and predestined them for the person, while being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, and with sins is sin. And it was answered by what al-Sa'd said: that disbelief and sins are decreed and predestined (maqdi and muqaddar), not the decree and predestination themselves; what it is obligatory to be pleased with is only the decree and predestination, not the decreed and predestined. But it is objected that there is no meaning to being pleased with the decree and predestination except being pleased with the decreed and predestined. And what al-Khayali verified in his gloss is that disbelief and sins have two aspects: the aspect of their being decreed and predestined by God, and the aspect of their being acquired (muktasaban) by the servant. So one must be pleased with them from the first aspect, not the second. And know that although faith in predestination is obligatory, it is not permissible to argue by it before occurrence in order to reach it, such as a person saying 'God has predestined for me zina, for example,' intending thereby to fall into zina, or after occurrence to escape the prescribed punishment or the like, such as a person who committed zina and said 'God predestined that for me,' intending thereby to escape the punishment. As for arguing by it after occurrence merely to ward off blame, there is no harm in it. In the authentic hadith: 'Indeed, the soul of Adam met the soul of Moses, peace be upon them, and Moses said to Adam: You are the father of mankind, you were the cause of your children's expulsion from Paradise by your eating from the tree. Adam said: O Moses, you are the one who...'
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage addresses the practical implications of belief in qada and qadr. It begins by stating that faith in the divine decree and predestination requires being pleased (rida) with them. An objection is raised: if one must be pleased with qada and qadr, that would include being pleased with disbelief and sins, since God decreed them. But being pleased with disbelief is itself disbelief, and with sins is sin. The answer from al-Sa'd is that disbelief and sins are the decreed things (maqdi, muqaddar), not the decree itself (qada, qadr). One is only required to be pleased with the decree, not the decreed. However, this answer is itself critiqued because being pleased with the decree necessarily means being pleased with the decreed. Then al-Khayali's verification is presented: disbelief and sins have two aspects: (1) as decreed and predestined by God, and (2) as acquired (iktisab) by the servant. One must be pleased with them from the first aspect but not the second. The passage then discusses the use of qadr as an excuse: although belief in qadr is obligatory, it cannot be used to justify committing a sin before the fact or to escape punishment after the fact. However, using it after the act merely to deflect blame (not to avoid legal punishment) is permissible. This is supported by the hadith of Adam and Moses, where Adam used qadr to rebut Moses' blame for the expulsion from Paradise.

Key Terms
الرضا
القضاء
القدر
المقضي
المقدور
الاكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being pleased with qadr means approving of all actions, including sin.
Correction: One must be pleased only with God's decree, not with the servant's acquisition of sin.

Checkpoint
According to al-Khayali's verification, from which aspect must one be pleased with disbelief and sins?
A) From the aspect of their being acquired by the servant.
B) From the aspect of their being decreed and predestined by God.
C) From both aspects equally.
D) From neither aspect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Khayali's two-aspect solution preserves the obligation to be pleased with qadr while rejecting approval of sin.

Using Qadr as an Excuse
Clarifies when using qadr as an excuse is allowed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when it is permissible and impermissible to use divine predestination as an excuse for one's actions.

Core Idea
Qadr is not a valid excuse for sin before or after the fact.

Belief in qadr does not justify committing sins or escaping punishment. This lesson explains the three scenarios: using qadr to justify a sin beforehand, using it to avoid punishment afterwards (both forbidden), and using it merely to deflect blame (permissible). The hadith of Adam and Moses illustrates the latter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three distinct rulings: before occurrence, after occurrence to escape punishment, and after occurrence to deflect blame.
The hadith is cut off but the key point is clear.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The passage lists: (1) using qadr to justify sin before committing it (forbidden), (2) using it after sin to avoid legal punishment (forbidden), (3) using it after sin only to ward off blame (permissible).
This is a clear classification.
3
Understand the hadith illustration
Adam used qadr to rebut Moses' blame for the expulsion from Paradise. This is an example of the permissible use: after the fact, to deflect blame, not to avoid a legal punishment.
The hadith shows that even prophets used this reasoning.

The Obligation to Be Pleased with Qada' and Qadar and the Problem of Sin
Sanusi states the obligation of being pleased with qada' and qadar and presents an objection based on kufr and sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that faith in divine decree requires being pleased with it, and the objection that this would entail being pleased with disbelief and sin.

Core Idea
Faith in decree demands pleasure with decree, but does that force us to be pleased with sin?

Sanusi states a foundational principle: believing in Allah's decree and predestination necessitates being pleased with them both. But a sharp objection immediately arises: if we must be pleased with whatever Allah decrees, that seems to include the acts of disbelief and sin that He decrees for people. Yet being pleased with disbelief is itself disbelief — a clear contradiction. This lesson presents the principle and the objection; the resolution will follow in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle carefully
The author states that faith in qada' and qadar requires being pleased with them. Notice the logical connection: faith naturally leads to contentment.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that this obligation leads to the absurdity of having to be pleased with kufr and sin. Why is that a problem? Because being pleased with kufr is itself kufr.
This is the dilemma: either you reject the obligation or you accept being pleased with sin.
3
Consider the dilemma
Ask yourself: How can we resolve this? The lesson ends with the objection unanswered. Reflect on what a possible answer might be before moving to Lesson 2.
The answer will involve a distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإيمان بالقضاء والقدر يستدعي الرضا بهما، فيجب الرضا بالقضاء والقدر، واستُشكل بأنَّه يلزم على ذلك الرضا بالكفر والمعاصي؛ لأنَّ الله قضى بهما وقدرهما على الشخص، مع أن الرضا بالكفر كفر، وبالمعاصي معصية.
وأجيب بما قاله السعد من أنَّ الكفر والمعاصي مقضي ومُقدَّرٌ لا قضاءٌ وقَدَر، والواجب الرضا به إنَّما هو القضاء والقدر لا المقضي والمقدور، وفيه أنه لا معنى للرضا بالقضاء والقدر إلا الرضا بالمقضي والمقدور، والذي حققه الخيالي في حاشيته : أنَّ الكفر والمعاصي لهما جهتان :
جهة كونهما مَقْضِيَيْنِ ومُقَدَّرَيْنِ الله .
وجهه: كونهما مُكْتَسَبَيْنِ للعبد، فيجب الرضا بهما من الجهة الأولى دون الثانية .
Faith in qada' and qadar necessitates being pleased with them, so it is obligatory to be pleased with qada' and qadar. It was objected that this entails being pleased with disbelief and sins, because Allah has decreed and predestined them for the person, despite the fact that being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, and with sins is sin. It was answered with what al-Sa'd said: that disbelief and sins are 'decreed' and 'predestined' (maqdi wa muqaddar), not 'decree' and 'destiny' (qada' wa qadar), and what is obligatory to be pleased with is only the decree and destiny, not the decreed and predestined. [Then an objection: there is no meaning to being pleased with decree and destiny except being pleased with the decreed and predestined.] What al-Khayali verified in his gloss is: disbelief and sins have two aspects: the aspect of being decreed and predestined by Allah, and the aspect of being acquired by the servant. So it is obligatory to be pleased with them from the first aspect, not the second.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its resolution regarding the obligation to be pleased with divine decree (qada') and predestination (qadar). The author first states the principle: faith in these two necessitates being pleased with them. An objector raises the problem: if we must be pleased with qada' and qadar, that would include being pleased with the acts of disbelief and sin that Allah decrees, but being pleased with disbelief is itself disbelief and being pleased with sin is sinful. The answer, attributed to al-Sa'd, distinguishes between the decree itself (qada' and qadar) and the decreed things (maqdi and muqaddar). What is obligatory is to be pleased with Allah's decree and predestination as His actions, not with the content of what is decreed when it is an act of the servant. However, the author notes that this distinction is problematic because being pleased with the decree implies being pleased with what is decreed. The verified solution by al-Khayali is to note that disbelief and sins have two aspects: from the perspective of being decreed by Allah, they are part of His creation and wisdom, so one must be pleased with Allah's decree (i.e., accept His divine ordering); from the perspective of being acquired by the servant, they are blameworthy acts, and one must not be pleased with the servant's choice of sin. So the pleasure is only with the divine decree, not with the servant's acquisition.

Correction: This passage shows that there is a problem, but the resolution will clarify that we distinguish between the act of decree and the decreed act's moral quality.

Resolving the Objection: Distinguishing Decree from Decreed and Acquisition
Sanusi presents al-Sa'd's attempted solution, its critique, and al-Khayali's verified resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sa'd's distinction between the decree and the decreed, its shortcoming, and al-Khayali's solution that considers the two aspects of an act.

Core Idea
We are pleased with Allah's decree, not with the servant's acquisition of sin.

The objection from the previous lesson left us with a dilemma. Now Sanusi presents two attempts at a resolution. First, al-Sa'd argues that we are only required to be pleased with the decree itself (qada' and qadar), not with the particular decreed acts (maqdi and muqaddar). But this is problematic because being pleased with the decree necessarily involves being pleased with what is decreed. The final and verified solution comes from al-Khayali: every sinful act has two aspects — it is decreed by Allah (so we accept His decree) and it is acquired by the servant (so we dislike the servant's choice). This resolves the dilemma without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Sa'd's answer
Al-Sa'd distinguishes between qada'/qadar (the decree) and maqdi/muqaddar (the decreed). The obligation applies only to the former. See if this solves the problem.
2
Notice the objection to al-Sa'd
The author points out that being pleased with the decree implies being pleased with the decreed. So al-Sa'd's answer is insufficient.
This shows that a more nuanced answer is needed.
3
Understand al-Khayali's two aspects
Al-Khayali explains that kufr and sins have two aspects: as decreed by Allah (we accept the divine decree) and as acquired by the servant (we dislike the sinful choice). So we are pleased with them from one aspect, not the other.
This distinguishes between divine ordainment and human responsibility.
4
Reflect on the solution
Ask yourself: Does this satisfy the objection? Why is it valid to be pleased with the decree but not with the acquisition?
The key is that pleasure is with Allah's action (creation of the act) not with the servant's action (acquisition).

Faith in qada' and qadar necessitates being pleased with them, so it is obligatory to be pleased with qada' and qadar. It was objected that this entails being pleased with disbelief and sins, because Allah has decreed and predestined them for the person, despite the fact that being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, and with sins is sin. It was answered with what al-Sa'd said: that disbelief and sins are 'decreed' and 'predestined' (maqdi wa muqaddar), not 'decree' and 'destiny' (qada' wa qadar), and what is obligatory to be pleased with is only the decree and destiny, not the decreed and predestined. [Then an objection: there is no meaning to being pleased with decree and destiny except being pleased with the decreed and predestined.] What al-Khayali verified in his gloss is: disbelief and sins have two aspects: the aspect of being decreed and predestined by Allah, and the aspect of being acquired by the servant. So it is obligatory to be pleased with them from the first aspect, not the second.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its resolution regarding the obligation to be pleased with divine decree (qada') and predestination (qadar). The author first states the principle: faith in these two necessitates being pleased with them. An objector raises the problem: if we must be pleased with qada' and qadar, that would include being pleased with the acts of disbelief and sin that Allah decrees, but being pleased with disbelief is itself disbelief and being pleased with sin is sinful. The answer, attributed to al-Sa'd, distinguishes between the decree itself (qada' and qadar) and the decreed things (maqdi and muqaddar). What is obligatory is to be pleased with Allah's decree and predestination as His actions, not with the content of what is decreed when it is an act of the servant. However, the author notes that this distinction is problematic because being pleased with the decree implies being pleased with what is decreed. The verified solution by al-Khayali is to note that disbelief and sins have two aspects: from the perspective of being decreed by Allah, they are part of His creation and wisdom, so one must be pleased with Allah's decree (i.e., accept His divine ordering); from the perspective of being acquired by the servant, they are blameworthy acts, and one must not be pleased with the servant's choice of sin. So the pleasure is only with the divine decree, not with the servant's acquisition.

When Can We Cite Predestination as an Excuse?
The author explains when it is permissible to cite predestination as an excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct application of citing divine decree (qadar) as an excuse — when it is forbidden and when it is permissible.

Core Idea
Citing qadar is allowed only to deflect blame, not to commit sin or escape punishment.

This lesson addresses a common question: Can a Muslim use predestination as an excuse for sin? The author sets clear boundaries, distinguishing between its use before sin, after sin for punishment, and after sin for blame. A famous hadith of Adam and Moses illustrates the permissible case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the three scenarios the author presents.
Pay attention to the phrases 'before occurrence', 'after occurrence to escape punishment', and 'after occurrence to deflect blame'.
2
Distinguish the three scenarios
Identify each scenario: (1) using qadar before a sin to reach it, (2) after a sin to escape punishment, (3) after a sin to deflect blame. Determine which are forbidden and which permissible.
The author explicitly states that only the third is permissible.
3
Study the hadith proof
Read the hadith of Adam and Moses. Notice how Adam uses qadar to deflect Moses' blame, not to justify his action or avoid any punishment. The Prophet confirms Adam's argument.
This hadith is the key evidence for the permissible use.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the wisdom behind these rulings. Why is it important to distinguish these uses? How does this protect against fatalism while preserving belief in qadar?
This connects to a common misunderstanding about Islam and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه وإن وجب الإيمان بالقدر لكن لا يجوز الاحتجاج به قبل الوقوع توصلاً إليه بأن قال شخص : قدَّر الله عليَّ الزنا، مثلاً ؛ وغرضه بذلك التوصل إلى الوقوع في الزنا، أو بعد الوقوع تخلصاً من الحد أو نحوه، بأن وقع شخص في الزنا مثلاً، وقال : قَدَّر الله عليَّ ذلك، وغرضه به التخلص من الحد، وأما الاحتجاج به بعد الوقوع لدفع اللَّومِ عنه فقط فلا بأس به، ففي الحديث الصحيح: «إنَّ روح آدم التقت مع روح موسى عليهما الصلاة والسلام، فقال موسى لآدم: أنت أبو البشر الذي كنتَ سبباً لإخراج أولادِكَ من الجنَّةِ بأكلك من الشجرة، فقال آدم: يا موسى؛ فأنت الذي اصطفاك الله بكلامه، وخط لك التوراة بيده تلومني على أمر قد قدَّرَهُ اللهُ عليَّ قبل أن يخلقني بأربعين سنةً، قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : فحَجَّ آدم موسى» أي : غلبه بالحجَّةِ.
Know that although faith in predestination (qadar) is obligatory, it is not permissible to use it as an argument before the occurrence (of a sin) to reach it — by a person saying, for example, 'Allah has predestined upon me zina' intending thereby to reach the act of zina — nor after the occurrence to escape the prescribed punishment or the like, such as a person committing zina and saying, 'Allah predestined that upon me' intending thereby to escape the punishment. But using it after the occurrence solely to deflect blame is permissible. In the authentic hadith: 'The spirit of Adam met the spirit of Moses, peace be upon them. Moses said to Adam: You are the father of mankind, you were the cause of your children being expelled from Paradise by eating from the tree. Adam said: O Moses, you are the one whom Allah chose with His speech and wrote the Torah for you with His hand; do you blame me for a matter that Allah had predestined upon me forty years before He created me? The Prophet said: So Adam prevailed over Moses,' i.e., defeated him by argument.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage addresses the legitimate and illegitimate uses of appealing to predestination (qadar). The author first states that it is forbidden to cite predestination before committing a sin in order to justify or facilitate it, e.g., saying 'Allah predestined zina for me' as an excuse to commit it. Similarly, it is forbidden to cite it after the sin to escape punishment (hadd) or legal consequences. However, it is permissible to cite it after the sin merely to deflect blame or criticism from others, not to avoid punishment. The author supports this with the well-known hadith of Adam and Moses. In the hadith, Adam uses predestination as a defense against Moses' blame for his eating from the tree, and the Prophet confirms that Adam was correct in doing so. This shows that using qadar to deflect blame after the fact is acceptable, as it acknowledges one's own action but attributes the ultimate decree to Allah.

Correction: The hadith only supports using qadar to deflect blame, not to justify sin or escape punishment. The author explicitly forbids those other uses.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Ash'ari and Maturidi Definitions of Qadar and Qada'
The author defines qadar and qada' according to Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools define qadar and qada' and how these definitions relate to attributes of action and essence.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, qadar is an action; for Maturidis, it is knowledge—and the opposite holds for qada'.

The concepts of qada' and qadar (divine decree and predestination) are central to Islamic theology, but their precise definitions differ between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. In this passage, the author clarifies each school's position, showing how qadar is linked to either action (Ash'ari) or knowledge (Maturidi), while qada' takes the opposite classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of qadar
Notice how Ash'aris define qadar as bringing into existence (attribute of action) while Maturidis define it as pre-eternal determination (attribute of knowledge).
This difference leads to opposite classifications of qadar as temporal or eternal.
2
Read the definitions of qada'
Contrast the Ash'ari view (pre-eternal will, attribute of essence) with the Maturidi view (creation with perfection, attribute of action).
The positions on qada' are the reverse of those on qadar.
3
Note the temporal implications
Understand that for Ash'aris, qadar is temporal and qada' is eternal; for Maturidis, it is the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ الأشاعرة والماتريدية اختلفوا في كل من القدر والقضاء : فالقَدَر عند الأشاعرة إيجاد الله الأشياء على قدرٍ مخصوص ووجه معين أراده تعالى، فيرجع عندهم لصفة فعلٍ ؛ لأنَّه عبارة عن الإيجاد، وهو من صفات الأفعال. وعند الماتريدية: تحديدُ اللهِ أزلاً كل مخلوق بحده الذي يوجد عليه من حسن وقُبْحٍ ونَفْعِ وضُرٍّ إلى غير ذلك؛ أي: علمه تعالى أزلاً صفات المخلوقات، فيرجعُ عندهم لصفة العلم وهي من صفات الذات. والقضاء عند الأشاعرة: إرادة الله الأشياء في الأزل على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزالُ فهو من صفات الذات عندهم . وعند الماتريدية : إيجاد الله الأشياء مع زيادة الإحكام والإتقان، فهو صفة فعل عندهم، فالقَدَر حادث والقضاء قديمٌ عند الأشاعرة، ولا كذلك عند الماتريدية. وقد حمل الشارحُ كلام المصنفِ على مذهب الماتريدية في القدر والقضاء دون مذهب الأشاعرة؛ لأنَّ القضاء في اللغة له نحو معانٍ سبعة أشهرها الحكم، وهو يرجع للفعل، فناسب أن يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل. وأمَّا القَدَر فلم يرد أن معناه في اللغة الفعل فناسب ألا يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل بل بالعلم، وقد نظم العلامة الأجهوري معنى القضاء والقدر، وحكى فيه الخلاف على غير هذا الوجه، فقال: [من الرجز] إرَادَةُ اللهِ مَعَ التَّعَلُّقِ فِي أَزَلٍ قَضَاؤُهُ فَحَفَّقِ وَالقَدَرُ الإيجادُ لِلأَشْيَا عَلَى وَجْهِ مُعَيَّنٍ أَرَادَهُ عَلا وَبَعْضُهُمْ قَدْ قَالَ: مَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ العِلْمُ مَعْ تَعَلَّةٍ فِي الأَزَلِ وَالقَدَرُ الإِبْجَادُ لِلأُمُورِ عَلَى وَفَانِ عِلْمِهِ المَذْكُورِ فأنت تراه جعل القضاء هو الإرادة مع التعلُّقِ الأزلي على القول الأول، أو العلم
Know that the Ash'aris and Maturidis differed regarding each of qadar and qada'. According to the Ash'aris, qadar is Allah's bringing into existence of things upon a specific measure and a particular manner that He willed. Thus it reverts, according to them, to an attribute of action, because it is an expression of bringing into existence, which is among the attributes of actions. According to the Maturidis, it is Allah's pre-eternal determination of every created thing according to its limit upon which it comes to exist, of good and evil, benefit and harm, etc.; that is, His pre-eternal knowledge of the attributes of creatures, so it reverts, according to them, to an attribute of knowledge, which is among the attributes of essence. Qada', according to the Ash'aris, is Allah's willing of things in pre-eternity as they will be in perpetuity, so it is, according to them, an attribute of essence. According to the Maturidis, it is Allah's bringing things into existence with the addition of precision and perfection, so it is an attribute of action according to them. Thus, according to the Ash'aris, qadar is temporal (hadith) and qada' is eternal (qadim), unlike the Maturidis. The commentator [the author of Tuhfat al-Mureed] interpreted the statement of the author [of Jawharat al-Tawhid] according to the Maturidi school concerning qadar and qada', not the Ash'ari school, because qada' in language has about seven meanings, the most famous of which is 'judgment', and that reverts to action, so it is appropriate that it be interpreted in technical terminology as action. As for qadar, its linguistic meaning has not been reported as action, so it is appropriate that it not be interpreted as action in terminology but rather as knowledge. And the scholar al-Ajhuri composed poetry on the meaning of qada' and qadar, relating the disagreement in another manner, saying: [in rajaz] 'His qada' is Allah's will with its pre-eternal relation, so understand; and qadar is bringing things into existence upon a specific manner that He willed. And some said: the meaning of the first is knowledge with a pre-eternal relation, and qadar is the bringing into existence of matters according to His mentioned knowledge.' You see that he made qada' to be will with pre-eternal relation according to the first statement, or knowledge.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of qadar and qada' and explains the rationale for the commentator's preference. The author first states that the two schools differ on both terms. For Ash'aris, qadar is Allah's act of creating things according to a specific measure and manner, so it is an attribute of action (i.e., temporal). For Maturidis, qadar is Allah's pre-eternal determination of each creature's attributes, based on His knowledge, so it is an attribute of essence (i.e., eternal). Conversely, qada' for Ash'aris is Allah's pre-eternal will of things as they will be, hence an attribute of essence; for Maturidis, it is the act of creation with perfection, hence an attribute of action. Thus, for Ash'aris, qadar is temporal and qada' is eternal, while for Maturidis the opposite. The commentator (author of Tuhfat al-Mureed) interprets the base poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) according to the Maturidi view, because the linguistic meaning of qada' is 'judgment' which suggests action, so it fits to interpret it as an action (Maturidi). Qadar does not linguistically mean action, so it is better interpreted as knowledge (Maturidi). Finally, al-Ajhuri's poem summarizes two opinions: one that qada' is eternal will and qadar is creation, and another that qada' is eternal knowledge and qadar is creation according to that knowledge.

Key Terms
قدر
قضاء
أشاعرة
ماتريدية
صفة فعل
صفة ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that both schools agree on qadar and qada' being attributes of the same kind.
Correction: In fact, Ash'aris see qadar as an action (temporal) and qada' as essence (eternal), while Maturidis reverse this.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, qadar is an attribute of:
A) Essence (dhat)
B) Action (fi'l)
C) Knowledge ('ilm)
D) Will (irada)

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of qadar and qada' are opposite: for Ash'aris, qadar is action and qada' is essence; for Maturidis, qadar is essence and qada' is action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience something you initially label as 'bad', do you instinctively attribute it to God's pre-eternal plan (as Maturidi qadar suggests) or to His direct creation in the moment (as Ash'ari qadar suggests)? What does your default reaction reveal about your heart's trust in God's wisdom?

The Ash'aris view qada' as God's eternal will—what if that will includes events that seem unjust to you? Are you willing to submit to that will, or do you secretly hold back? Take a moment to repent if needed.

The Commentator's Preference and Al-Ajhuri's Poem on Qada' and Qadar
The commentator explains his preference for Maturidi definitions and presents a poetic summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator of Jawharat al-Tawhid preferred the Maturidi definitions and how al-Ajhuri summarized the two opinions in verse.

Core Idea
The commentator chooses Maturidi definitions because qada' linguistically means action, while qadar does not.

After presenting the two schools' positions, the commentator explains his decision to interpret the base poem according to the Maturidi school. He supports his choice with linguistic analysis and then cites a poetic summary by al-Ajhuri that encapsulates the two views.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's rationale
Understand why the commentator prefers the Maturidi interpretation: qada' linguistically means 'judgment' (action), so it fits to interpret it as an action (Maturidi view). Qadar does not linguistically mean action, so it is interpreted as knowledge (Maturidi view).
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
Note that the commentator uses the linguistic meaning of qada' (hukm) to argue it should be an attribute of action, while qadar has no such linguistic basis.
3
Read al-Ajhuri's poem
The poem presents two opinions: one where qada' is pre-eternal will and qadar is creation, and another where qada' is pre-eternal knowledge and qadar is creation according to that knowledge.
This shows the diversity of scholarly views on these terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ الأشاعرة والماتريدية اختلفوا في كل من القدر والقضاء : فالقَدَر عند الأشاعرة إيجاد الله الأشياء على قدرٍ مخصوص ووجه معين أراده تعالى، فيرجع عندهم لصفة فعلٍ ؛ لأنَّه عبارة عن الإيجاد، وهو من صفات الأفعال. وعند الماتريدية: تحديدُ اللهِ أزلاً كل مخلوق بحده الذي يوجد عليه من حسن وقُبْحٍ ونَفْعِ وضُرٍّ إلى غير ذلك؛ أي: علمه تعالى أزلاً صفات المخلوقات، فيرجعُ عندهم لصفة العلم وهي من صفات الذات. والقضاء عند الأشاعرة: إرادة الله الأشياء في الأزل على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزالُ فهو من صفات الذات عندهم . وعند الماتريدية : إيجاد الله الأشياء مع زيادة الإحكام والإتقان، فهو صفة فعل عندهم، فالقَدَر حادث والقضاء قديمٌ عند الأشاعرة، ولا كذلك عند الماتريدية. وقد حمل الشارحُ كلام المصنفِ على مذهب الماتريدية في القدر والقضاء دون مذهب الأشاعرة؛ لأنَّ القضاء في اللغة له نحو معانٍ سبعة أشهرها الحكم، وهو يرجع للفعل، فناسب أن يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل. وأمَّا القَدَر فلم يرد أن معناه في اللغة الفعل فناسب ألا يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل بل بالعلم، وقد نظم العلامة الأجهوري معنى القضاء والقدر، وحكى فيه الخلاف على غير هذا الوجه، فقال: [من الرجز] إرَادَةُ اللهِ مَعَ التَّعَلُّقِ فِي أَزَلٍ قَضَاؤُهُ فَحَفَّقِ وَالقَدَرُ الإيجادُ لِلأَشْيَا عَلَى وَجْهِ مُعَيَّنٍ أَرَادَهُ عَلا وَبَعْضُهُمْ قَدْ قَالَ: مَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ العِلْمُ مَعْ تَعَلَّةٍ فِي الأَزَلِ وَالقَدَرُ الإِبْجَادُ لِلأُمُورِ عَلَى وَفَانِ عِلْمِهِ المَذْكُورِ فأنت تراه جعل القضاء هو الإرادة مع التعلُّقِ الأزلي على القول الأول، أو العلم
Know that the Ash'aris and Maturidis differed regarding each of qadar and qada'. According to the Ash'aris, qadar is Allah's bringing into existence of things upon a specific measure and a particular manner that He willed. Thus it reverts, according to them, to an attribute of action, because it is an expression of bringing into existence, which is among the attributes of actions. According to the Maturidis, it is Allah's pre-eternal determination of every created thing according to its limit upon which it comes to exist, of good and evil, benefit and harm, etc.; that is, His pre-eternal knowledge of the attributes of creatures, so it reverts, according to them, to an attribute of knowledge, which is among the attributes of essence. Qada', according to the Ash'aris, is Allah's willing of things in pre-eternity as they will be in perpetuity, so it is, according to them, an attribute of essence. According to the Maturidis, it is Allah's bringing things into existence with the addition of precision and perfection, so it is an attribute of action according to them. Thus, according to the Ash'aris, qadar is temporal (hadith) and qada' is eternal (qadim), unlike the Maturidis. The commentator [the author of Tuhfat al-Mureed] interpreted the statement of the author [of Jawharat al-Tawhid] according to the Maturidi school concerning qadar and qada', not the Ash'ari school, because qada' in language has about seven meanings, the most famous of which is 'judgment', and that reverts to action, so it is appropriate that it be interpreted in technical terminology as action. As for qadar, its linguistic meaning has not been reported as action, so it is appropriate that it not be interpreted as action in terminology but rather as knowledge. And the scholar al-Ajhuri composed poetry on the meaning of qada' and qadar, relating the disagreement in another manner, saying: [in rajaz] 'His qada' is Allah's will with its pre-eternal relation, so understand; and qadar is bringing things into existence upon a specific manner that He willed. And some said: the meaning of the first is knowledge with a pre-eternal relation, and qadar is the bringing into existence of matters according to His mentioned knowledge.' You see that he made qada' to be will with pre-eternal relation according to the first statement, or knowledge.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of qadar and qada' and explains the rationale for the commentator's preference. The author first states that the two schools differ on both terms. For Ash'aris, qadar is Allah's act of creating things according to a specific measure and manner, so it is an attribute of action (i.e., temporal). For Maturidis, qadar is Allah's pre-eternal determination of each creature's attributes, based on His knowledge, so it is an attribute of essence (i.e., eternal). Conversely, qada' for Ash'aris is Allah's pre-eternal will of things as they will be, hence an attribute of essence; for Maturidis, it is the act of creation with perfection, hence an attribute of action. Thus, for Ash'aris, qadar is temporal and qada' is eternal, while for Maturidis the opposite. The commentator (author of Tuhfat al-Mureed) interprets the base poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) according to the Maturidi view, because the linguistic meaning of qada' is 'judgment' which suggests action, so it fits to interpret it as an action (Maturidi). Qadar does not linguistically mean action, so it is better interpreted as knowledge (Maturidi). Finally, al-Ajhuri's poem summarizes two opinions: one that qada' is eternal will and qadar is creation, and another that qada' is eternal knowledge and qadar is creation according to that knowledge.

Key Terms

قضاء
أشاعرة
ماتريدية
إرادة
علم
إيجاد
تحديد

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer the Maturidi interpretation of qada'?
A) Because it is the majority view
B) Because qada' linguistically means 'judgment', an action
C) Because the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid was Maturidi
D) Because the Ash'ari definition is contradictory

Key Takeaway
The commentator prefers Maturidi definitions because qada' linguistically means action, while qadar does not, and al-Ajhuri's poem summarizes two opinions on qada' and qadar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life's events—both the good and the difficult—do you see them as part of God's qada' and qadar? How does knowing that qada' means His will (action) and qadar means His knowledge change the way you trust His decree? This week, pause before complaining about a hardship and remind yourself: this is from His knowledge and will. Will you do that?
?
The commentator chose a definition based on careful linguistic analysis rather than mere tradition. Have you ever examined the Arabic roots of key Aqeedah terms to deepen your understanding? Pick one term from this lesson (like 'qada' or 'irada') and look up its linguistic meanings in a classical dictionary. Commit to doing that this week.

Supplication and Predestination: Ahl al-Sunnah vs. Mu'tazila
Compares Ahl al-Sunnah and Mu'tazila on du'a's benefit despite predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah position on the benefit of supplication despite divine predestination and how the Mu'tazila differ while remaining within the fold of Islam.

Core Idea
Supplication benefits even though all things are decreed; wrong interpretations don't always mean disbelief.

The relationship between supplication (du'a) and divine predestination (qadar) has been a point of theological debate. This passage presents the Ahl al-Sunnah view that du'a is beneficial despite Allah's complete decree, then contrasts it with the Mu'tazila position that du'a does not change the decree. Importantly, the author clarifies that the Mu'tazila are not considered disbelievers for this view because they interpret the relevant Quranic verses allegorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Note the two sections: first the Ahl al-Sunnah view, then the Mu'tazila view.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage first explains the Ahl al-Sunnah view: all things are determined in Allah's knowledge, but this does not negate the need for supplication, just as one does not abandon eating despite divine decree for satiety. Then it presents the Mu'tazila view: they deny that supplication benefits in changing the decree.
3
Understand the key nuance about takfir
Notice that the author says the Mu'tazila do not become disbelievers because they did not deny the Quran but interpreted it allegorically. This is a crucial point about how theological errors are judged.
This shows the author's balanced approach: refuting without excommunicating.
4
Reflect on the practical implication
Ask yourself: Why does the author compare du'a to eating? What does this analogy teach about human responsibility and divine decree?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحفوظ، وأما بحسب العلم فجميع الأشياء مُبرمةٌ ؛ لأنه إن علم الله حصول المُعلَّق عليه حصل المُعلَّق ولا بدَّ ، وإن علم الله عدم حصوله، لم يحصل ولا بد، لكن لا يترك الشخص الدعاء اتكالاً على ذلك كما لا يترك الأكل اتكالاً على إبرام الله الأمر في الشبع.
وأما عند المعتزلة فالدعاء لا ينفع، ولا يكفرون بذلك ؛ لأنهم لم يكذبوا القرآن كقوله تعالى : ادْعُونِي أَسْتَجِبْ لَكُم [غافر: ٦٠] ، بل أولوا الدعاء بالعبادة، والإجابة بالثواب .
The preserved [decree], and as for according to knowledge, all things are determined; because if Allah knows the occurrence of the conditioned thing, the conditioned thing occurs necessarily, and if Allah knows its non-occurrence, it does not occur necessarily. But a person does not abandon supplication relying on that, just as he does not abandon eating relying on Allah's decreeing the matter of satiety. As for according to the Mu'tazila, supplication does not benefit, and they do not become disbelievers because of that; because they did not deny the Qur'an like His saying: 'Call upon Me, I will respond to you' [Ghafir:60], but rather they interpreted supplication as worship, and response as reward.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a comparison of two theological positions on supplication (du'a) in relation to divine predestination. The author first states the Ahl al-Sunnah position: all things are determined in Allah's eternal knowledge, but this does not negate the benefit of supplication, just as one does not abandon eating despite Allah's decree for satiety. Then he contrasts the Mu'tazila view: they deny that supplication benefits in changing divine decree, but they do not become disbelievers because they interpret the Quranic verses differently (e.g., 'du'a' as worship, 'ijaba' as reward). This shows the author's careful approach: refuting the Mu'tazila without declaring them disbelievers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the benefit of supplication makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: The author clarifies that the Mu'tazila, who deny the benefit of du'a, are not disbelievers because they interpret the relevant Quranic verses allegorically, thus avoiding outright denial.

Checkpoint
