Key Takeaway
Supplication is beneficial despite Allah's complete decree, and theological errors that rely on allegorical interpretation do not automatically lead to excommunication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Qur'anic and Prophetic Evidence for the Benefit of Supplication
Evidence from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus that du’a benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus establish that supplication brings benefit, and why the Qur'an is specified as the primary proof.

Core Idea
The Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus all affirm that du’a is beneficial.

The author begins by unpacking the poetic line that refers to the Qur'an's promise. He then cites two key verses and emphasizes that the Qur'an is the most authoritative proof due to its widespread transmission. He also notes that the Prophet's practice and the consensus of the community reinforce the same truth. This lesson examines these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice the grammatical breakdown of the poetic line and then the two Quranic verses cited.
The passage is dense; take your time with the grammar.
2
Identify the two Quranic verses
Find Ghafir:60 and Baqarah:186. What promise do they convey? Understand that they are cited as direct evidence.
These verses contain commands and promises of response.
3
Understand why the Qur'an is specified
The author says the Qur'an is specified due to its tawatur (widespread transmission), not because other sources lack evidence. This implies that the Sunnah and consensus also prove the benefit of du'a.
This refutes the misconception that only the Qur'an is evidence.
4
Note the mention of Sunnah and consensus
The Prophet's supplication at Badr and the agreement of the early community are mentioned as additional proofs. Reflect on how they corroborate the Qur'anic evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَمَا مِنَ القُرْآنِ وَعْداً يُسْمَعُ) أي : لأجل الذي يسمع داله من ألفاظ القرآن حال كونه موعوداً به، فالكاف للتعليل، و(ما) اسم موصول، و(يسمع) صلته، و(وعداً) بمعنى موعوداً به حال، والمسموع إنما هو الدال والموعود به المدلول لا الدال ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَقَالَ رَبُّكُمُ ادْعُونِي أَسْتَجِبْ لَكُم [غافر: ٦٠]، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا سَأَلَكَ عِبَادِى عَنِي فَإِنِّي قَرِيبٌ أُجِيبُ دَعْوَةَ الدَّاعِ إِذَا دَعَانِ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٦]، وتخصيص القرآن لتواتره لا لقصر الدلالة عليه، وإلا فيدل على أن الدعاء ينفع السنة والإجماع، فقد دعا ربَّه في مواطن كثيرة كيوم بدر، وقد أجمع عليه السلف والخلف .
واعلم أن الإجابة تتنوّع ؛ فتارةً يقع المطلوب بعينه على الفور، وتارة يقع ولكن يتأخر لحكمة فيه، وتارةً تقع الإجابة بغير المطلوب حيث لا يكون في المطلوب مصلحة ناجزة، وفي ذلك الغير مصلحة ناجزة، أو يكون في المطلوب مصلحة، وفي ذلك الغير أصلح منها على أن الإجابة مقيدة بالمشيئة، كما يدل عليه قوله تعالى : فَيَكْشِفُ مَا تَدْعُونَ إِلَيْهِ إِن شَاءَ ﴾ [الأنعام: (٤١) فهو مقيّد لإطلاق الآيتين السابقتين؛ فالمعنى : ادعوني أستجب لكم إن شئت، وأجيب دعوة الداعي إذا دعاني إن شئت.
His saying: (like from the Qur'an a promise heard) meaning: because of that whose indicator is heard from the words of the Qur'an in the state of being promised by it. The kaf is for causation, and (ma) is a relative pronoun, and (yusma'u) is its relative clause, and (wa'dan) is in the sense of 'promised' as a circumstantial accusative, and the heard thing is only the indicator, while the promised thing is the indicated, not the indicator. Allah the Exalted said: 'And your Lord said: Call upon Me, I will respond to you' [Ghafir:60], and Allah the Exalted said: 'And when My servants ask you about Me, indeed I am near. I respond to the supplication of the supplicant when he calls upon Me' [Al-Baqarah:186]. And specifying the Qur'an is due to its tawatur, not due to shortcoming of evidence upon it. Otherwise, it indicates that supplication benefits according to the Sunnah and consensus. He (the Prophet) supplicated to his Lord in many places like the day of Badr, and the predecessors and successors agreed upon it.
And know that the response varies; sometimes the requested thing itself occurs immediately, sometimes it occurs but is delayed for a wisdom in it, and sometimes the response occurs with something other than the requested where there is no immediate benefit in the requested, while in that other there is immediate benefit, or there is benefit in the requested but in that other there is something more beneficial, while acknowledging that the response is conditioned upon the will, as indicated by His saying: 'Then He removes what you call upon to if He wills' [Al-An'am:41]. So it restricts the absoluteness of the two preceding verses. So the meaning is: Call upon Me, I will respond to you if I will, and I respond to the supplication of the supplicant when he calls upon Me if I will.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage serves as a proof that supplication benefits, based on the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus. The author explains the poetic line 'كما من القرآن وعداً يسمع' grammatically and then cites two Qur'anic verses (Ghafir:60 and Baqarah:186) as evidence. He notes that the specification of the Qur'an is due to its tawatur (widely transmitted), not because other sources lack evidence. Then he mentions that the Prophet and the early community agreed on the benefit of supplication. Then he discusses the nature of response: it may be immediate, delayed, or substituted with something better. Finally, he clarifies that all these are conditioned on Allah's will, as shown by the verse from Al-An'am:41, which restricts the absolute promise in the earlier verses. So supplication benefits, but within the framework of Allah's will.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only the Qur'an provides evidence for the benefit of supplication.
Correction: The Qur'an is specified because of its tawatur, but the Sunnah and consensus also prove the benefit, as the author explicitly states.

Key Takeaway

?

How Supplication Is Answered: Immediate, Delayed, or Substituted, All by Allah's Will
Classification of divine response and its conditioning on Allah's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different ways Allah may respond to supplication and that all responses are conditioned on His will.

Core Idea
Allah’s response to du’a comes in various forms, always subject to His wisdom and will.

The passage then delves into the nature of divine response (ijabah). It explains that the requested thing may be granted immediately, delayed for a reason, or replaced with something better. Crucially, all of this depends on Allah's will, as one verse restricts the absolute promise of response. This lesson clarifies these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'واعلم أن الإجابة تتنوّع' to the end. Notice the classification of response types and the final conditioning verse.
This part clarifies misconceptions about unconditional response.
2
Identify the three types of response
The passage lists: 1) the requested thing itself occurs immediately, 2) it occurs but is delayed for a wisdom, 3) the response occurs with something other than the requested, either because the requested lacks immediate benefit or because the substitute is more beneficial.
These cover the possibilities of how du'a is answered.
3
Understand the condition of will
The author states that all response is conditioned upon Allah's will, citing Al-An'am:41 which says 'if He wills'. This verse restricts the absolute promise in Ghafir:60 and Baqarah:186.
This shows that divine wisdom may choose not to grant the exact request even if the du'a is sincere.
4
Reflect on the overall message
The passage teaches that supplication is beneficial, but the specific outcome is always within Allah's perfect knowledge and will. This balances hope and submission.

قوله : (كَمَا مِنَ القُرْآنِ وَعْداً يُسْمَعُ) أي : لأجل الذي يسمع داله من ألفاظ القرآن حال كونه موعوداً به، فالكاف للتعليل، و(ما) اسم موصول، و(يسمع) صلته، و(وعداً) بمعنى موعوداً به حال، والمسموع إنما هو الدال والموعود به المدلول لا الدال ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَقَالَ رَبُّكُمُ ادْعُونِي أَسْتَجِبْ لَكُم [غافر: ٦٠]، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا سَأَلَكَ عِبَادِى عَنِي فَإِنِّي قَرِيبٌ أُجِيبُ دَعْوَةَ الدَّاعِ إِذَا دَعَانِ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٦]، وتخصيص القرآن لتواتره لا لقصر الدلالة عليه، وإلا فيدل على أن الدعاء ينفع السنة والإجماع، فقد دعا ربَّه في مواطن كثيرة كيوم بدر، وقد أجمع عليه السلف والخلف .
واعلم أن الإجابة تتنوّع ؛ فتارةً يقع المطلوب بعينه على الفور، وتارة يقع ولكن يتأخر لحكمة فيه، وتارةً تقع الإجابة بغير المطلوب حيث لا يكون في المطلوب مصلحة ناجزة، وفي ذلك الغير مصلحة ناجزة، أو يكون في المطلوب مصلحة، وفي ذلك الغير أصلح منها على أن الإجابة مقيدة بالمشيئة، كما يدل عليه قوله تعالى : فَيَكْشِفُ مَا تَدْعُونَ إِلَيْهِ إِن شَاءَ ﴾ [الأنعام: (٤١) فهو مقيّد لإطلاق الآيتين السابقتين؛ فالمعنى : ادعوني أستجب لكم إن شئت، وأجيب دعوة الداعي إذا دعاني إن شئت.
His saying: (like from the Qur'an a promise heard) meaning: because of that whose indicator is heard from the words of the Qur'an in the state of being promised by it. The kaf is for causation, and (ma) is a relative pronoun, and (yusma'u) is its relative clause, and (wa'dan) is in the sense of 'promised' as a circumstantial accusative, and the heard thing is only the indicator, while the promised thing is the indicated, not the indicator. Allah the Exalted said: 'And your Lord said: Call upon Me, I will respond to you' [Ghafir:60], and Allah the Exalted said: 'And when My servants ask you about Me, indeed I am near. I respond to the supplication of the supplicant when he calls upon Me' [Al-Baqarah:186]. And specifying the Qur'an is due to its tawatur, not due to shortcoming of evidence upon it. Otherwise, it indicates that supplication benefits according to the Sunnah and consensus. He (the Prophet) supplicated to his Lord in many places like the day of Badr, and the predecessors and successors agreed upon it.
And know that the response varies; sometimes the requested thing itself occurs immediately, sometimes it occurs but is delayed for a wisdom in it, and sometimes the response occurs with something other than the requested where there is no immediate benefit in the requested, while in that other there is immediate benefit, or there is benefit in the requested but in that other there is something more beneficial, while acknowledging that the response is conditioned upon the will, as indicated by His saying: 'Then He removes what you call upon to if He wills' [Al-An'am:41]. So it restricts the absoluteness of the two preceding verses. So the meaning is: Call upon Me, I will respond to you if I will, and I respond to the supplication of the supplicant when he calls upon Me if I will.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage serves as a proof that supplication benefits, based on the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus. The author explains the poetic line 'كما من القرآن وعداً يسمع' grammatically and then cites two Qur'anic verses (Ghafir:60 and Baqarah:186) as evidence. He notes that the specification of the Qur'an is due to its tawatur (widely transmitted), not because other sources lack evidence. Then he mentions that the Prophet and the early community agreed on the benefit of supplication. Then he discusses the nature of response: it may be immediate, delayed, or substituted with something better. Finally, he clarifies that all these are conditioned on Allah's will, as shown by the verse from Al-An'am:41, which restricts the absolute promise in the earlier verses. So supplication benefits, but within the framework of Allah's will.

Key Terms
الدعاء
الإجابة
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That all supplications are answered exactly as requested and immediately.
Correction: The passage explains that response may be delayed, or substituted with something better, and always depends on Allah's will.

Checkpoint

A) The Sunnah
B) The verse from Al-An'am
C) The consensus
D) The Prophet's practice

Key Takeaway
Allah's response to du'a may be immediate, delayed, or substituted, and is always conditioned on His infinite wisdom and will.

The Number and Functions of Angels Appointed to Every Person
Hadith enumerating angels per person and their duties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific numbers and roles of the angels appointed over every human being according to a hadith narrated by 'Uthman.

Core Idea
Twenty angels guard each person day and night, each with a unique duty.

This lesson examines a detailed hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) describes the exact number of angels assigned to each human being and their specific functions. The hadith, reported by 'Uthman, reveals the comprehensive care and recording that surrounds every individual. We will explore the roles of these angels and note variations in narrations.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the angelic assignments.
Notice the symmetry: ten by night and ten by day.
2
List the angels and their positions
Identify each angel mentioned: on right, left, front, back, sides, grasping forelock, on lips, and the tenth protector.
The hadith also mentions additional narrations with twenty or four hundred angels.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why each person is assigned so many angels—indicating constant recording, protection, and support.
This connects to the broader topic of divine care and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حديث : لا تدخل الملائكة بيتاً فيه جرس (۳) ، ونحوه فالمراد ملائكة الرحمة، وقد د أن عثمان سأل النبي الا عن عدد الملائكة الموكلين بالآدمي، فقال: «لكل آدمي عشرة بالليل وعشرة بالنهار : واحدٌ عن يمينه وآخرُ عن شماله واثنان بين يديه ومن خلفه، واثنان على جنبيه (4) ، وآخر قابض على ناصيته، فإن تواضع رفعه، وإن تكبر وضعه، واثنان على شفتيه ليس يحفظانِ عليه إلا الصلاة على النبي ، والعاشر يحرسه من الحيةِ أن تدخل فاه (٥) ، وفي بعض الروايات أنه ذكر عشرين ملكاً، وذكر الأُبي أنه يحفظ لابن عطية أن كل آدمي يُوكَّل به من حين وقوعه نطفة في الرحم إلى موته أربع مئة ملك ؛ وحفظهم للعبد إنما هو من المعلَّق، وأما المبرم فلا بد من إنفاذه، فيتنحون عنه حتى ينفذ.
قوله : ( وكَاتِبُونَ) عطفٌ على قوله : (حافظون).
وقوله : ( خِيْرَةُ) بالرفع صفة لقوله : (كاتبون)؛ أي: مختارون؛ لأن الله تعالى اختارهم لذلك، وقد علمت أنه وقع خلاف في هذا العطف، فقيل: للتغاير، وقيل: للتفسير، والحق الأول، والمراد بالجمع ما فوق الواحد ؛ لأن كل واحد من العباد إنما عليه ملكان وكل منهما رقيب؛ أي حافظ وعتيد ؛ أي: حاضر ، لا كما قد يُتوهم
As for the hadith: 'Angels do not enter a house in which there is a bell' and the like, the intended [angels] are the angels of mercy. And it has been reported that 'Uthman asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about the number of angels appointed over the human being, so he said: 'For every human being there are ten by night and ten by day: one on his right and another on his left, two in front of him and behind him, two on his sides, another grasping his forelock; if he humbles himself, he raises him, and if he is arrogant, he lowers him; and two on his lips, guarding nothing upon him except the prayer upon the Prophet, and the tenth protects him from the snake entering his mouth.' In some narrations, he mentioned twenty angels. And al-Ubbi mentioned that he preserves from Ibn 'Atiyyah that every human being is entrusted from the time he falls as a drop in the womb until his death with four hundred angels. And their protection of the servant is only regarding the contingent (mu'allaq); as for the decreed (mubram), there is no escape from its execution, so they withdraw from him until it is executed. His saying: 'and writers' is a conjunction upon his saying: 'guardians'. And his saying: 'chosen' is in the nominative as an adjective of his saying: 'writers', i.e., selected, because Allah chose them for that. And you have known that there occurred disagreement concerning this conjunction; it is said: for differentiation, and it is said: for explanation, and the truth is the first. And the intended by the plural is more than one, because every one of the servants has two angels upon him, each of them a watcher (raqib), i.e., a guardian ('atiyya), i.e., present, not as might be imagined.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage discusses the hadith that angels do not enter houses with bells, clarifying that this refers to angels of mercy. It then presents a specific hadith in which 'Uthman asks the Prophet about the number of angels appointed over each human being. The Prophet responds by detailing twenty angels: ten by night and ten by day, each with specific duties: one on the right and one on the left (recording good and bad deeds), two in front and two behind (protection), two on the sides (perhaps additional protection), one grasping the forelock (rewarding humility and punishing arrogance), two on the lips (only guarding the prayer upon the Prophet), and the tenth protecting from snakes entering the mouth. The passage also notes that some narrations mention twenty angels, and al-Ubbi reports from Ibn 'Atiyyah that each person has four hundred angels from conception to death. Then the passage makes a crucial theological distinction: the angels' protection applies only to contingent matters (mu'allaq) that can change, not to the decreed (mubram) that must happen; in such cases, the angels withdraw until the decree is executed. Finally, the passage returns to the grammatical explanation of the poem, clarifying that 'katibun' (writers) is a conjunction on 'hafizun' (guardians), and that the correct interpretation is that they are distinct (taqhayur), and each person has two angels, each a watcher (raqib) and ready ('atiyya).

عتيد

Correction: The hadith reveals twenty angels with diverse roles, including guardians and those on lips.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You are protected by angels from snakes entering your mouth. What 'snakes'—harmful words, gossip, slander—might slip past your lips? How will you guard your tongue knowing the other angels are recording for the Day of Account?

Angelic Protection: Contingent vs Decreed Fate
Distinguishes angelic protection for contingent vs decreed fate and grammatical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between contingent (mu'allaq) and decreed (mubram) matters and how angels protect only the contingent.

Core Idea
Angels protect from what can change, not from what is decreed.

This lesson explores a crucial theological distinction within the hadith on angels. While angels are appointed to protect and record, their protection applies only to contingent events—those that can be altered by supplication or good deeds. Decreed matters, on the other hand, must occur, and the angels withdraw during their execution. We will also examine the grammatical discussion in the poem regarding the terms 'writers' and 'guardians.'

Guided Path
1
Understand the key terms
Learn the meaning of 'mu'allaq' (contingent) and 'mubram' (decreed).
These terms are central to Islamic theology of qadar.
2
Read the passage on protection
Read the Arabic and translation to see how the angels' protection is limited.
Note that the angels withdraw when a decree is executed.
3
Examine the grammatical analysis
Review the commentary on 'katibun' and 'hafizun' and the debate on whether they refer to the same or different groups.
This clarifies that 'writers' are a distinct category from 'guardians'.

The Death of the Killed Person and Divine Decree
Al-Ujhuri expounds the Ahl al-Sunna view on the death of the killed person and its epistemic status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine that every killed person dies at his appointed time solely by Allah's decree, and the epistemological distinction between before and after the event.

Core Idea
The killer is not the cause of death; Allah's eternal decree determines the moment of death.

This passage from al-Ujhuri's commentary addresses a key theological question: does a killer actually cause the death of his victim? Ahl al-Sunna affirms that every death, including that of a killed person, occurs exactly at the time Allah has decreed. This lesson explains the doctrine and its implications for understanding divine foreknowledge and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once silently. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the passage first states the doctrinal position, then discusses the epistemic situation.
Pay attention to the terms 'qisas' (retribution) and 'kash' (acquisition).
2
Identify the core doctrine
Find the sentence that states the Ahl al-Sunna view. It says every killed person dies with the expiration of his lifespan and the arrival of his appointed time, by Allah's creation, without any involvement of the killer.
The killer is only subject to retribution due to his acquisition (kash).
3
Understand the epistemic nuance
Read the second part about what would happen if the person had not been killed. Note that Ahl al-Sunna say it is possible he would live or die at that time, because we do not know Allah's knowledge. But after the killing, it becomes clear that Allah knew his death at that time, so he would have died anyway.
This shows certainty after the fact, but epistemic humility beforehand.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this doctrine upholds divine sovereignty while maintaining human accountability. The killer is punished for his act of killing (kash), not for causing death per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فمختار أهل السنة أن كل مقتول ميت بانقضاء عمره، وحضور أجله في الوقت الذي علم الله حصول موته فيه أزلاً، بخلقه تعالى من غير مدخلية للقاتل فيه، وإنما وجب عليه القصاص نظراً للكسب فقط ؛ وعند أهل السنة أنه لو لم يقتل لجاز أن يموت في ذلك الوقت وألا يموت فيه ؛ لأنه لا اطلاع لنا على ما في علم الله، فيحتمل أنه لو لم يقتل أن يموت في ذلك الوقت، إن لم يكن عمره في علم الله أكثر من ذلك، ويحتمل ألا يموت فيه إن كان عمره في علم الله أكثر
(٤)
ذلك، وهذا التجويز ذاتي (1) على فرض عدم قتله، كما هو ظاهر، وإلا فقد بان(٢) بقتله أن الله علم بموته في ذلك الوقت، فلا يتخلف
(4) (أنه) ساقطة من (أ) و(ط)، وكلمة (فيحتمل) غير واضحة، وتقرأ فيحمل)
(1) قوله: (وهذا التجويز ذاتي إلخ) مراده أن جواز الأمرين - وهما الحياة والموت بلا قتل - مرتب على عدم القتل، وأما التجويز الذي هو حكم أهل السنة بالجواز فهو بعد القتل قطعاً. أجهوري. انتهى
In summary, the chosen view of Ahl al-Sunna is that every killed person dies with the expiration of his lifespan and the arrival of his appointed time at the moment that Allah knew his death would occur from eternity, by His creation, without any involvement of the killer in it. The killer is only subject to retribution due to his acquisition (kash). According to Ahl al-Sunna, if he had not been killed, it would be possible that he would die at that time and possible that he would not die at that time, because we have no knowledge of what is in Allah's knowledge. So it is possible that if he had not been killed, he would die at that time, if his lifespan in Allah's knowledge is not more than that, and possible that he would not die at that time if his lifespan in Allah's knowledge is more. (4) (The word 'annahu' is missing from copies A and T, and the word 'fayahmalu' is unclear, and it is read as 'fayuhmalu'.) That, and this possibility (1) is inherent based on the assumption of not killing him, as is apparent. Otherwise, it becomes clear by his killing that Allah knew his death at that time, so it does not fail to occur.
(1) His statement: 'This possibility is inherent...' — his intention is that the permissibility of the two matters — life and death without killing — is contingent upon the absence of killing. As for the permissibility that is the ruling of Ahl al-Sunna regarding possibility, it is after the killing definitively. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the core doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna regarding death and killing. The author states that every killed person dies exactly at his appointed time (ajal) as determined by God's eternal knowledge. The killer is not the true cause of death; rather, God creates the death at that moment. Nonetheless, the killer is held accountable for his act of killing (kash), which is why retribution (qisas) is required. The author then explains the epistemological stance: before the person is killed, we cannot be certain whether he would have died at that same time if not killed, because God's knowledge is hidden from us. After the killing, however, we know that God's knowledge included his death at that moment, so if he had not been killed, he would have died anyway. The footnote clarifies that the possibility of life or death is only on the assumption of not killing; after the fact, Ahl al-Sunna are certain that if not killed, he would have died, due to God's knowledge.

Key Terms
المقتول
الأجل
العلم الأزلي
القصاص
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Sunna are uncertain about divine decree or that the killer truly causes death.
Correction: The passage shows that Ahl al-Sunna are certain about the decree after the event, and they deny that the killer causes death; death is solely by Allah's creation.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, after a person is killed, what is the ruling regarding whether he would have died if not killed?
A) He would have lived
B) He would have died
C) It remains possible
D) It is unknown

Key Takeaway
Every death, including murder, happens exactly at Allah's appointed time; the killer is accountable due to his acquisition, not for causing death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever held a grudge against someone for a harm they caused you, how does knowing that Allah alone decreed the event challenge that bitterness? What is one specific grudge you will release today as an act of tawakkul?
?
Your death has a single, unchangeable moment appointed by Allah. Are you living as if that moment is certain? Name one thing you are postponing in your spiritual life that you would do today if you knew your ajal is tomorrow.
?
The killer is accountable for his act even though Allah creates the death. Where in your life do you use the excuse of qadr to avoid responsibility for your sins? Write down one sin you rationalize with 'It was decreed anyway' and then commit to repenting sincerely, knowing your kasb is your own.

Three Mu'tazila Views on the Death of a Killed Person
Commentator enumerates three Mu'tazila positions on the term of a slain person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct Mu'tazila positions on whether a killed person has one or two terms (ajal), and how Ahl al-Sunna differ from them.

Core Idea
A killed person: one term or two? The Mu'tazila split into three answers.

The commentator introduces three different Mu'tazila schools on the relationship between killing and death. Each offers a distinct logic concerning the term (ajal) of a killed person. By examining these positions, we see the Ash'ari emphasis on divine decree and the single, fixed term for every soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice how the commentator structures the three views.
The passage begins with a general statement then lists the three views.
2
Identify each of the three views
Find al-Ka'bi's view, the majority view, and Abu al-Hudhayl's view. Note how each defines the term of a killed person.
Al-Ka'bi proposes two terms; the others propose one term but differ on its timing.
3
Contrast with Ahl al-Sunna
The passage concludes by stating that the difference between the Mu'tazila views and Ahl al-Sunna becomes apparent. Think about the Ash'ari position on qadar.
Ahl al-Sunna hold that every person has a single, fixed term determined by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وغَيْرُ هَذَا بَاطِلٌ لا يُقْبَلُ أي وغير ما ذكر من مذاهب المخالفين لأهل السنة غير مطابق للواقع، لا يقبل عند العقلاء المتمسكين بالحق. وأشار المصنف بذلك للردّ على أهل الاعتزال، فإن لهم مذاهب ثلاثة : الأول مذهبُ الكعبي : وهو أن المقتول ليس بميت؛ لأنَّ القتل فعل العبد، والموت فعله تعالى، واستدل على ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَين مُتُمْ أَوْ قُتِلْتُمْ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٥٨]، فإن العطف يقتضي المغايرة، وأهل السنة يقولون المعنى : ولئن مُتُّم من غير سبب أو قتلتم بأن مُتُّم بسبب؛ فعند الكعبي أن المقتول له أجلان أجل بالقتل، وأجل بالموت، فلو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أجله بالموت. والثاني مذهب جمهورهم وهو أن القاتل قطع على المقتول أجله، فعندهم أن المقتول له أجل واحدٌ؛ وهو الوقت الذي علم الله موته فيه لولا القتل، فلو لم يُقتل لعاش إليه قطعاً. والثالث مذهب أبي الهذيل : وهو أن المقتول أجله في ذلك الوقت فقط، فعنده أن المقتول له أجل واحدٌ؛ وهو الوقت الذي قتل فيه، فلو لم يُقتل لمات بدل القتل قطعاً، وبهذا التقرير ظهر الفرقُ بين مذاهب المعتزلة ومذهب أهل السنة فتدبر .
His saying: 'And other than this is false and unacceptable' means: any view other than what has been mentioned of the doctrines of those who oppose Ahl al-Sunna is not in conformity with reality and is not accepted by rational people who adhere to truth. The author alluded by this to refute the Mu'tazila, for they have three doctrines: The first is the doctrine of al-Ka'bi: that the killed person is not dead, because killing is the act of the servant and death is the act of God, and he adduced as evidence His saying: 'And if you die or are killed' (Quran 3:158), for the conjunction implies difference. Ahl al-Sunna say the meaning is: 'If you die without cause or are killed, meaning you died due to a cause.' So according to al-Ka'bi, the killed person has two terms: a term by killing and a term by death; if he had not been killed, he would have lived until his term by death. The second is the doctrine of their majority: that the killer cuts off the term of the killed; according to them, the killed person has one term only, which is the time God knew he would die were it not for the killing; if he had not been killed, he would have lived until it definitely. The third is the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl: that the term of the killed person is only at that time; according to him, the killed person has one term only, which is the time he was killed; if he had not been killed, he would have died instead of killing definitely. And by this exposition the difference between the doctrines of the Mu'tazila and the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna becomes apparent, so ponder.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a comparative exposition of the Mu'tazila positions on the death of a killed person and the Ash'ari response. It begins by stating that any view other than that of Ahl al-Sunna is false and unacceptable. Then it identifies that the author is refuting the Mu'tazila, who have three schools. First, al-Ka'bi's view: the killed person is not dead because killing is the act of the servant and death is God's act, citing verse 3:158. Ahl al-Sunna interpret the verse differently. Al-Ka'bi holds that the killed person has two terms: one by killing and one by death. Second, the majority of Mu'tazila: the killer cuts off the term of the killed, so they have one term: the time God knew he would die if not killed. Third, Abu al-Hudhayl: the killed person's term is only the time he is killed; if not killed, he would have died at that same time anyway. The commentator concludes that this clarifies the difference between the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna.

أبو الهذيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Mu'tazila all agree on the issue of the slain person's term.
Correction: In fact, they have three distinct positions: al-Ka'bi (two terms), the majority (one term interrupted), and Abu al-Hudhayl (one term at the moment of killing).

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, how many terms (ajal) does a killed person have in the view of al-Ka'bi?
A) One term
B) Two terms
C) No term
D) Three terms

Key Takeaway
The three Mu'tazila views show diversity within the school on qadar, but all differ from Ahl al-Sunna's belief that every person has a single fixed term determined by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of someone being murdered, do you truly believe their lifespan was exactly what Allah decreed, or does a part of you feel that their life was cut short unjustly? How does this lesson challenge your heart to submit to Allah's decree even in tragedy?
?
The Mu'tazila view that killing is purely a human act separate from God's action might comfort a sense of control. Have you ever secretly wished to have that control over your own life's end? What does that wish reveal about your trust in Allah's plan?
?
If you were killed tomorrow, would you die feeling that your life was incomplete or that Allah's decree for you was perfectly fulfilled? How does embracing the Ahl al-Sunna belief of one fixed term change how you live today?

Divine Decree
beginner
4 min
Three Mu'tazilite Views on the Term of the Killed Person
The author presents three Mu'tazilite views on the killed person's term and contrasts them with Ahl al-Sunna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct Mu'tazilite positions on the term (ajal) of a killed person and how they differ from the Sunni stance.

Core Idea
Three Mu'tazilite schools disagreed on the killed person's term—showing the depth of their rationalist theology.

Before delving into the Sunni position on divine decree, this passage clarifies the Mu'tazilite backdrop. The author presents three competing views within Mu'tazilism regarding whether a killed person has one term or two. Understanding these differences sharpens our appreciation of the Sunni doctrine that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وبهذا التقرير ظهر الفرق' — this signals the key takeaway.
2
Identify each of the three Mu'tazilite views
The passage lists three views: al-Ka'bi's two-term view, the majority's one-term view (death postponed), and Abu al-Hudhayl's one-term view (death simultaneous). Find each and note how they differ.
Notice how each view defines 'if he were not killed' differently.
3
Understand the contrast with Ahl al-Sunna
The passage ends by stating that this exposition clarifies the difference from Sunni doctrine. Reflect on what that difference might be based on the Mu'tazilite assumptions.
The Sunni position (not detailed here) holds that all terms are fixed by God's decree alone.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why these distinctions matter: they reveal how Mu'tazilites rationalized divine justice and human action. Ask yourself: does the killer truly cut short a life, or is the time of death already decreed?
This will prepare you for the Sunni response in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو قتلتم بأن مُتُّم بسبب ; فعند الكعبي أن المقتول له أجلان أجل بالقتل، وأجل بالموت، فلو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أجله بالموت . والثاني مذهب جمهورهم وهو أن القاتل قطع على المقتول أجله، فعندهم أن المقتول له أجل واحدٌ ; وهو الوقت الذي علم الله موته فيه لولا القتل، فلو لم يُقتل لعاش إليه قطعاً . والثالث مذهب أبي الهذيل : وهو أن المقتول أجله في ذلك الوقت فقط، فعنده أن المقتول له أجل واحدٌ ; وهو الوقت الذي قتل فيه، فلو لم يُقتل لمات بدل القتل قطعاً، وبهذا التقرير ظهر الفرقُ بين مذاهب المعتزلة ومذهب أهل السنة فتدبر .
Or you were killed by a cause; according to al-Ka'bi, the killed person has two terms: a term by killing and a term by death; if he were not killed, he would live until his term by death. The second is the doctrine of their majority: that the killer cuts off the term of the killed; according to them, the killed has one term, which is the time God knew he would die if not for the killing; if he were not killed, he would definitely live until it. The third is the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl: that the killed person's term is only that time; according to him, the killed has one term, which is the time he was killed; if he were not killed, he would have died at that time instead of the killing. With this exposition, the difference between the doctrines of the Mu'tazila and the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna becomes clear, so reflect.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents three Mu'tazilite views on the term (ajal) of a killed person, contrasting them with the Sunni position. The author first states a hypothetical: 'Or you were killed by a cause.' Then he enumerates: (1) Al-Ka'bi holds that the killed has two independent terms—one by killing and one by natural death; if not killed, he would live until the natural death term. (2) The majority of Mu'tazila hold that the killer cuts short the term; the killed has one term—the time God knew he would die if not killed; if not killed, he would definitely live until that term. (3) Abu al-Hudhayl holds that the killed's term is only the time of killing; if not killed, he would die at that same time instead. The passage then concludes that this exposition clarifies the difference between Mu'tazilite doctrines and the Sunni doctrine, inviting the reader to reflect.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage shows three distinct positions within Mu'tazilism, demonstrating their rationalist diversity.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Mu'tazila mentioned, what happens if the killed person had not been killed?
A) He would die at the same time as the killing.
B) He would live until the term God knew he would die if not killed.
C) He would live to a different term assigned for natural death.
D) He would die immediately.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazilites disagreed on whether a killed person has one or two terms, but all three views contrast with the Sunni affirmation of one fixed decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a tragic death, do you ever wonder if the person was 'supposed' to die then? The Mu'tazilites debated whether killing cuts off a term. How does your belief about God's decree affect the way you grieve? Take a moment to recall a loss; did your theology give you peace or struggle?
?
Al-Ka'bi said the killed person has two possible terms, depending on circumstances. Do you live as if your life has multiple possible outcomes, or do you trust that every moment is exactly decreed? When you make choices, do you feel the weight of God's knowledge?
?
The majority of Mu'tazila said the killer 'cuts off' the term. If you have ever harmed someone – even unintentionally – have you considered the weight of your actions against God's decree? How does knowing that God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities change how you seek forgiveness?
